Talaq — Divorce
beginner
8 min
Shams al-A'imma and the ʻIddah of Umm Walad
Anecdote showing Shams al-A'imma's superior fiqh knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling that an umm walad must observe ʻiddah after being freed before remarrying, and identify the two errors Shams al-A'imma corrected.

Core Idea
After freeing, an umm walad must observe ʻiddah before marriage.

This biographical anecdote highlights the deep fiqh understanding of Shams al-A'imma. When the sultan married the freed ummahat al-awlad to his servants, the scholars approved, but Shams al-A'imma pointed out a crucial oversight: after freeing, they are in ʻiddah and cannot marry immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first action
The sultan married the ummahat al-awlad to free servants while they were still slaves. Identify the problem: the servants already had free wives, so marrying slave women over free women is invalid.
Based on principle that marrying a slave over a free woman is not allowed unless conditions are met.
2
Identify the second action
The sultan then freed them and renewed the contract. Shams al-A'imma pointed out that after freeing, the ummahat are in ʻiddah and cannot marry until it expires.
The ʻiddah of an umm walad after freeing is one menstrual cycle according to Hanafis.
3
Recognize the lesson
The other scholars were made to forget the correct answer so that Shams al-A'imma's virtue would appear.
This story shows his superior knowledge of nuanced rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعلق بأم الولد حكاية لطيفة ذكرها في المعراج لما أخرج شمس الأئمة من السجن زوج السلطان أمهات الأولاد من خدامه الأحرار فسأل العلماء عن هذه فقالوا: نعم ما فعلت فقال الأئمة له أخطأت لأن تحت كل خادم حرة وهذا تزوج الأمة على الحرة فقال السلطان اعتقهن وأجدد العقد فسأل العلماء فقالوا: نعم ما فعلت. فقال شمس الأئمة له : أخطأت لأن العدة تجب عليهن بعد الإعتاق فكان المعتدة تزویج الغير، فأنسى الله من تعالى العلماء الجواب في هاتين المسألتين ليظهر فضل شمس الأئمة اهـ. ولكن حكاها محب الشحنة فيما كتبه على الهداية على غير هذا لوجه وهو أنه لما خطأه في الثانية أغراه عليه القاضي فحبسه وأن هذا كان سبب حبسه ، أن القاضي حينئذ كان فخر الإسلام البزدوي، وأن طلبته وعلماء عصره لا ينقطعون عنه ولا يتركون الاشتغال عليه فمنعوا عنه كتبه فأملى المبسوط من حفظه . وقيل : كان سبب حبسه أن السلطان أراد أن يأخذ من الرعية مظلمة كبيرة ثم ترك بعضها فمدحه القاضي فأنكر عليه شمس الأئمة فقال : لا يمدح إذا ترك جميعه فكيف بترك بعضه؟ فحبسه . وحكى شمس الأئمة في المبسوط واقعة مناسبة للموطوءة بشبهة دالة على أفضلية الإمام شمس الدين بن العلماء رضي الله تعالى عنه على علماء زمانه هي : رجل زوج ابنيه بنتين وعمل الوليمة وجمع وفيهم أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه لكنه لم يكن حينئذ من المشهورين ففي أثناء الليل سمعوا ولولة النساء فسألوا فأخبروا انهن غلطن فأدخلت زوجة كل أخ على أخيه فسألوا العلماء فأجابوا بأن كل واحد يجتنبها حتى تنقضي عدتها فتعود إلى زوجها فعسر ذلك الجواب فقال الإمام رضي الله عنه : يطلق كل زوجته ويعقد على موطوءته ويدخل عليها للحال لأنه صاحب العدة بعد ما سأل كل واحد من الأخوين عن مراده فقال كل مرادي موطوءتي لا المعقود عليها فرجع العلماء إلى جوابه. ثم رأيت بعد ذلك أن أعود إلى شرح المسألة الخلافية في أم الولد إذا لم تعلم كم بين موتهما توضيحاً للطلاب فقال في شرح المجمع وقالا : يجمع بين العدتين احتياطاً لجواز أن يكون المولى مات أولاً فعتقت ثم مات الزوج فوجب عليها عدة
And among what relates to the umm walad is a nice story mentioned in al-Miʻrāj: When Shams al-A'imma was released from prison, the sultan married the ummahat al-awlad (mothers of children) to his free servants, and he asked the scholars about this, and they said: 'Good what you did.' Shams al-A'imma said to him: 'You are mistaken, because under every servant there is a free woman, and this is marrying a slave woman over a free woman.' So the sultan freed them and renewed the contract, and asked the scholars, and they said: 'Good what you did.' Shams al-A'imma said: 'You are mistaken, because ʻiddah is obligatory upon them after freeing, so the one in ʻiddah would be marrying another.' Then Allah made the scholars forget the answer in these two issues to manifest the virtue of Shams al-A'imma. End. But Muḥibb al-Shiḥna narrated it in his commentary on al-Hidāya in a different way: that when he corrected him in the second issue, the judge incited the sultan against him and imprisoned him, and that this was the cause of his imprisonment, that the judge at that time was Fakhr al-Islām al-Bazdawī. And that his students and scholars of his time did not stop visiting him nor leave studying with him, so they prevented his books from him, and he dictated al-Mabsūṭ from memory. And it was said: The cause of his imprisonment was that the sultan wanted to take a great injustice from the subjects and then left some of it, and the judge praised him, but Shams al-A'imma rebuked him, saying: 'He is not praised when he leaves all, so how about leaving some?' So he imprisoned him. And Shams al-A'imma narrated in al-Mabsūṭ a story suitable about one who has intercourse by shubha, indicating the superiority of Imam Shams al-Dīn ibn al-ʻUlamā (may Allah be pleased with him) over the scholars of his time: A man married his two sons to two daughters and held a wedding feast and gathered people, and among them was Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him), but at that time he was not famous. During the night they heard the wailing of women, and they asked, and they were told that they had become confused; the wife of each brother was brought to the other. They asked the scholars, and they answered that each should avoid his wife until her ʻiddah expires, then return to her husband. That answer was difficult. Then Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: 'Let each divorce his wife and marry the one he actually had intercourse with, and enter upon her immediately, because he is the owner of the ʻiddah,' after asking each of the two brothers about his intention, and each said: 'My intended is the one I had intercourse with, not the one contracted to me.' So the scholars retracted their answer. Then I saw after that I should return to explaining the disputed issue of the umm walad when the time between their deaths is not known, as a clarification for students. He said in Sharḥ al-Majmaʻ: 'And they said: combine both ʻiddahs as a precaution, because it is possible that the master died first, so she became free, then the husband died, so multiple ʻiddah is obligatory.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is biographical, narrating two stories that illustrate the deep fiqh understanding of Hanafi scholars. The first story is about Shams al-A'imma (Imam al-Halwani?) who corrected the sultan and scholars regarding marrying ummahat al-awlad. Initially, the sultan married them to servants while they were still slaves, and scholars approved. Shams al-A'imma pointed out that the servants already had free wives, so marrying slave women over them is invalid. The sultan then freed them and renewed the contract, and again scholars approved. Shams al-A'imma pointed out that they are now in ʻiddah (after freeing) and cannot be married immediately. This shows his superior knowledge. The story also mentions a different version where this led to his imprisonment. The second story is about Imam Abu Hanifa, who was present at a wedding where the brides were mistakenly swapped between two brothers. The other scholars ruled that each must wait until the ʻiddah of the woman he had intercourse with expires, then return to his contracted wife. Abu Hanifa gave a more practical solution: each brother should express his intention (that he intended the one he had intercourse with), then divorce his contracted wife and marry the one he actually had intercourse with immediately, because he is the one who has the right to ʻiddah (since ʻiddah follows the man who had intercourse). This shows Abu Hanifa's quick application of fiqh principles. The author then briefly returns to the earlier issue of combining ʻiddahs when the order of deaths is unknown, citing Sharḥ al-Majmaʻ that the Sahibayn combine both ʻiddahs as precaution.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; upon the master's death, she becomes free upon his death.
عدة (ʻiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband, or after separation from her master; during which she cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Shams al-A'imma say the sultan's second action (freeing and then marrying) was wrong?
A) Because ummahat al-awlad cannot marry free men
B) Because they were in ʻiddah after being freed
C) Because the servants were not free
D) Because they were already married

Key Takeaway
After being freed, an umm walad must observe ʻiddah before she can marry again.

beginner

Abu Hanifa's Solution for Swapped Brides
Anecdote showcasing Abu Hanifa's quick application of fiqh principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of intention (niyya) and ʻiddah ownership in cases of mistaken intercourse (shubha).

Core Idea
In case of swapped brides, each man can marry the woman he intended immediately.

This famous anecdote illustrates Imam Abu Hanifa's legal acumen. At a wedding, brides were mistakenly swapped, and the scholars gave a difficult ruling. Abu Hanifa proposed a simple solution based on intention and the principle that the man who had intercourse has custody of the ʻiddah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Two brothers married two sisters. During the wedding, the brides were confused; each brother slept with the other's wife.
2
Initial ruling
The other scholars ruled that each brother must avoid the woman he slept with until her ʻiddah expires, then return to his contracted wife.
This ruling is based on the idea that intercourse creates a right (ʻiddah) that must be honored.
3
Identify the problem
This answer was difficult (ʻasara dhālik al-jawāb) because it caused prolonged separation and uncertainty.
4
Abu Hanifa's solution
Abu Hanifa asked each brother about his intention. Each said: 'I intended the woman I actually had intercourse with, not the one contracted to me.' Then he said: Let each divorce his contracted wife and marry the one he slept with immediately, because he is the owner of her ʻiddah.
The key is that the ʻiddah of the woman belongs to the man who had intercourse, so he can marry her without waiting period.
5
Outcome
The scholars retracted their answer and accepted Abu Hanifa's ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is biographical, narrating two stories that illustrate the deep fiqh understanding of Hanafi scholars. The first story is about Shams al-A'imma (Imam al-Halwani?) who corrected the sultan and scholars regarding marrying ummahat al-awlad. Initially, the sultan married them to servants while they were still slaves, and scholars approved. Shams al-A'imma pointed out that the servants already had free wives, so marrying slave women over them is invalid. The sultan then freed them and renewed the contract, and again scholars approved. Shams al-A'imma pointed out that they are now in ʻiddah (after freeing) and cannot be married immediately. This shows his superior knowledge. The story also mentions a different version where this led to his imprisonment. The second story is about Imam Abu Hanifa, who was present at a wedding where the brides were mistakenly swapped between two brothers. The other scholars ruled that each must wait until the ʻiddah of the woman he had intercourse with expires, then return to his contracted wife. Abu Hanifa gave a more practical solution: each brother should express his intention (that he intended the one he had intercourse with), then divorce his contracted wife and marry the one he actually had intercourse with immediately, because he is the one who has the right to ʻiddah (since ʻiddah follows the man who had intercourse). This shows Abu Hanifa's quick application of fiqh principles. The author then briefly returns to the earlier issue of combining ʻiddahs when the order of deaths is unknown, citing Sharḥ al-Majmaʻ that the Sahibayn combine both ʻiddahs as precaution.

Key Terms
موطوءة بشبهة (mawṭūʼa bi shubha): A woman with whom intercourse occurred due to a mistake (shubha), e.g., mistaking her for one's wife.
صاحب العدة (sāḥib al-ʻiddah): The person who has the right to the ʻiddah; here, the man who had intercourse has the right to marry the woman immediately because he is the one who caused the ʻiddah.

Why did the scholars retract their answer after hearing Abu Hanifa's solution?
A) Because Abu Hanifa was well-known

Key Takeaway
In cases of mistaken intercourse by shubha, the man who had intercourse can immediately marry the woman by stating his intention and divorcing the contracted wife.

Umm Walad's 'Iddah When Order of Deaths Unknown
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah combination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the combined 'iddah of an umm walad when the order of her master's and husband's deaths is unknown, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When order of deaths is unknown, precaution dictates combining two 'iddahs, but Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree on the timeframe.

This passage deals with a unique scenario: a female slave who has borne a child to her master (umm walad) is also married to a free man. If both her master and husband die and it is unknown who died first, how should she observe her 'iddah? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). Understanding this ruling requires careful reasoning about the legal consequences of each possible sequence of deaths and the application of the principle of precaution (ihtiyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
An umm walad is married to a free man. Both her master and her husband die, but it is unknown who died first and how much time elapsed between their deaths.
The umm walad occupies a special status: she is a slave but owes a different 'iddah upon the master's death than upon the husband's death.
2
Understand the two possible sequences
If the master died first: she is still married to her husband, so no 'iddah for master. Later when husband dies, she observes a free woman's 'iddah (4 months 10 days). If the husband died first: she observes a slave's 'iddah (2 months 5 days) from the husband, then later when master dies, she observes 3 menstruations.
3
Apply the precautionary principle
Since the order is unknown, precaution requires the stricter combination: the maximum possible period from each sequence. This leads to two views. Abu Hanifa says: combine 4 months 10 days (from first sequence) with 3 menstruations (from second sequence). The Sahibayn say: combine 2 months 5 days (from second sequence) with 3 menstruations.
The disagreement hinges on which 'iddah is considered to have been established with certainty.
4
Note the restriction to umm walad
This ruling applies only to an umm walad. A mudabbira (slave promised freedom) or ordinary slave woman has no 'iddah upon emancipation or master's death by consensus.
Also, the 'iddah of umm walad from her master is merely istibra' (ascertaining the womb), not a true 'iddah of marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخمسة أيام فعليها أن تعتد بأربعة أشهر وعشر فيها ثلاث حيض احتياطاً لأن المولى إن كان مات أولاً لم تلزمها عدته لأنها منكوحة وبعد موت الزوج يلزمها أربعة أشهر وعشر لأنها حرة، وإن مات الزوج أولاً لزمها شهران وخمسة أيام وقد انقضت عدتها منه لأنها مصورة أن بينهما هذه أو أكثر، فموت المولى بعده يوجب عليها ثلاث حيض فتجمع بينهما احتياطا . الثالث أن لا يعلم كم بين موتيهما ولا الأول منهما فكالأول عنده وكالثاني عندهما؛ كذا في المعراج وغيره. وقيد بأم الولد لأن المديرة والأمة إذا أعتقت أو مات سيدها لا عدة عليهما بالإجماع كما ذكره الاسبيجابي. وفي فروق الكرابيسي: المعتدة في عدة الزوج تغسل زوجها ولا تغسل مولاها في عدته إذا كانت أم ولد لأنها ليست عدة النكاح بل هي استبراء اهـ.
And five days, so she must observe an 'iddah of four months and ten days including three menstruations as a precaution, because if the master died first, his 'iddah is not incumbent on her as she is married, and after the husband's death, four months and ten days are incumbent on her because she is free. If the husband died first, two months and five days are incumbent on her and her 'iddah from him has concluded because it is understood that between them is this or more, then the master's death after that necessitates three menstruations, so she combines both as a precaution. Third: if it is not known how much time is between their deaths nor which died first, then according to him [Abu Hanifa] it is like the first case, and according to them [the Sahibayn] it is like the second; thus in al-Mi'raj and others. And it is restricted to umm walad because the mudabbira and the slave woman, if she is freed or her master dies, there is no 'iddah on them by consensus, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. And in Furuk al-Karabisi: The woman observing 'iddah from her husband washes her husband but does not wash her master during his 'iddah if she is umm walad, because it is not an 'iddah of marriage but rather istibra'. End.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the 'iddah of an umm walad (a slave concubine who bore a child to her master) when both her master and her husband die, and the order of deaths is unknown. Abu Hanifa holds that she must observe the longer 'iddah of four months and ten days (the 'iddah of widowhood for a free woman) combined with three menstruations (the 'iddah of an umm walad upon her master's death), as a precautionary measure. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that she must observe two months and five days (the period for a slave) combined with three menstruations. The text explains the reasoning: if the master died first, she is free and owes the full four months and ten days; if the husband died first, she owes only two months and five days. When the order is unknown, precaution requires the stricter combination. The passage then adds a qualification: this ruling applies only to umm walad, not to a mudabbira (a slave promised freedom upon master's death) or an ordinary slave woman, who have no 'iddah upon emancipation or master's death by consensus. It also quotes al-Karabisi that the umm walad observing 'iddah from her husband may wash him (a matter of ritual purification) but not her master, because the latter 'iddah is merely istibra' (ascertaining the womb) not a true 'iddah.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby acquiring certain rights including the obligation of 'iddah upon the master's death.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her spouse, during which she cannot remarry.
احتياط (ihtiyat): Precautionary measure taken when the actual legal ruling is uncertain, ensuring that the obligation is fulfilled in the strictest possible way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what 'iddah must an umm walad observe if the order of her master's and husband's death is unknown?
A) Four months and ten days only
B) Two months and five days only
C) Four months and ten days plus three menstruations
D) Three menstruations only

Key Takeaway
When the order of deaths is unknown, the umm walad must observe a combined 'iddah; Abu Hanifa's precautionary view (4 months 10 days + 3 menstruations) is the relied-upon position.

Sahibayn's Precautionary 'Iddah for Umm Walad When Order of Deaths Unknown
Continuation of intra-Hanafi dispute with detailed proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Sahibayn's argument for combining 'iddahs for the umm walad when the order of deaths is unknown, and contrast it with Abu Hanifa's position by identifying the role of the preventive factor (mani').

Core Idea
When the cause is certain but the preventive factor is in doubt, precaution requires applying the ruling.

The passage explores a specific intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the 'iddah of an umm walad (mother of the master's child) when both the master and her husband die in unknown order. The Sahibayn argue that she must observe both 'iddahs as a precaution. This lesson unpacks their reasoning using the concepts of cause (sabab), preventive factor (mani'), and the principle of precaution (ihtiyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the scenario
The dispute: When an umm walad's master (who owns her) and her husband die, and the order of deaths is unknown. Abu Hanifa (apparently) does not require combining 'iddahs, while the Sahibayn require her to observe both the 'iddah from the master (three menstruations) and from the husband (four months ten days).
The husband's right is a preventive factor (mani') for the master's 'iddah while the husband is alive.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof by analogy
They draw an analogy: If a man marries several women in one contract (or unknown order) and dies, all women must observe 'iddah because the cause (death) is certain and the preventive factor (which marriage was valid) is in doubt. Similarly, here the cause (death of both) is certain, but the preventive factor (who died first) is in doubt.
The analogy is based on the shared illa: certain cause, doubtful preventive factor.
3
Contrast with doubt about the cause itself
If the cause itself is in doubt, no precaution is taken. Example: conditional divorce where the condition's occurrence is unknown. The Sahibayn distinguish this from their case: here one probability (husband died after master) is certain, while the other is merely possible. The certain probability takes effect via precaution.
This is based on the principle that 'shubhat al-shubha' (doubt within a doubt) is disregarded.
4
Examine the critiques from later scholars
Fath al-Qadir notes that the Sahibayn's argument is 'shared obligation' (i.e., it can be applied equally to both sides). Al-Kafi states it is pure precaution, but Fath al-Qadir counters that precaution only applies after the cause is established.
These critiques show that the dispute is not settled; the Sahibayn's view is a preferred precaution, but not necessarily the final ruling.
5
Extract ancillary rulings from the passage
The passage also mentions: she has no inheritance from husband if her free status is uncertain; no difference between revocable and irrevocable divorce; and a paternity ruling when she remarries during 'iddah and gives birth.
These are related consequences of the uncertain status.

Then after that I saw that I should return to explaining the disputable issue regarding the umm walad when it is not known how much time is between their deaths, for clarification to students. He said in Sharh al-Majma': 'And they both (Sahibayn) said: She combines between the two 'iddahs as a precaution, because it is possible that the master died first, so she became free, then the husband died, so the 'iddah of death becomes obligatory on her; and it is possible that the husband died first and two months and five days passed, then the master died, so three menstruations become obligatory. This is because the master's death is the cause for 'iddah of three menstruations, and the existence of the husband's right is a preventive factor. Since the preventive factor remains in doubt, the ruling of the cause becomes obligatory as a precaution for her, just as if a man marries two women in one contract (and three in one contract, and four in one contract) and dies while it is unknown which marriage was first, then the 'iddah is obligatory for all of them because the cause exists and there is doubt about the preventive factor regarding differentiation, i.e., which marriage was earlier. This is unlike when there is doubt about the cause itself, for then precaution is not taken to establish the ruling because the ruling cannot be established without the cause. For example, if a man says, 'If I do not do such-and-such, then you are divorced' and then he dies and it is not known whether the condition occurred or not, she does not observe the 'iddah of divorce because there is doubt about the cause, since the divorce only occurs upon the existence of the condition, and its existence is doubtful. But here, the reality is only for one probability; however, one of the two probabilities is certain, and the other probability is merely possible. The explanation: The husband's death after the master's death certainly necessitates 'iddah of death—this probability is certain. The probability that the husband died before the master does not certainly necessitate 'iddah of three menstruations, because it is possible that the master's death was after the husband but before the expiry of two months and five days, in which case it is not obligatory, and it is possible that it was after the expiry of that period, so it becomes obligatory. Thus the probability is certain on one of the two estimates, not the other; therefore the probability that is certainly established takes the place of reality by way of precaution. The probability of obligation of 'iddah from the master is not established because it is a doubt within a doubt, which is legally disregarded by consensus, unlike the obligation of 'iddah on those women (in the analogy) because the probability of obligation of 'iddah on them is established: each marriage is either earlier or not; if it is earlier, 'iddah is certainly obligatory; otherwise it is certainly not. So the probability is established and is attached to reality.' End. And in Fath al-Qadir after the two proofs: 'It is not hidden that it is a shared objection.' In al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid: 'Indeed their (Sahibayn) opinion is precaution.' And in Fath al-Qadir: 'Precaution is only after the cause is apparent, because it is acting by the stronger of the two proofs.' Then he said in al-Kafi: 'She has no inheritance from her husband because we do not know that she was free on the day of his death.' End. And therein: 'There is no difference between her divorce being revocable or irrevocable in all aspects.' And therein also: 'If a man dies leaving his umm walad or frees her, and she gives birth to a child within two years, he is obligated; if she gives birth after more than two years, he is not obligated unless he claims it, then it is obligated.' End. And in al-Khaniyah: 'An umm walad who was freed by her master or he died and the 'iddah became incumbent on her, then she married during the 'iddah and gave birth to a child within two years from the time of the master's death or emancipation, and within six months from the time she married, and both claim the child, then the child belongs to the master according to their statement because of the 'iddah that was there.' He claims it.

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed reasoning for the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the umm walad's 'iddah when the order of deaths is unknown. It begins by quoting Sharh al-Majma', which explains the Sahibayn's view that she combines both 'iddahs as a precaution. The reasoning draws an analogy to a man who marries multiple women in one contract (or multiple contracts) and dies, and the order is unknown: all women must observe 'iddah because the cause (death) exists and the preventive factor (which marriage was valid) is in doubt. The text distinguishes this from cases where the cause itself is in doubt, such as a conditional divorce where the condition's occurrence is unknown—then no 'iddah is owed. The key point is that here one probability (that the husband died after the master) is certain, while the other is merely possible; certainty takes priority via precaution. Then the passage quotes Fath al-Qadir and al-Kafi, noting that the Sahibayn's view is based on precaution, but precaution applies only after the cause is known. Finally, it mentions ancillary rulings: no inheritance for her if her free status is uncertain, the same rule regardless of divorce type (revocable/irrevocable), and a ruling about paternity when the umm walad gives birth after remarrying during 'iddah. The final line from al-Khaniyah is incomplete.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani'): A preventive factor that blocks the application of a ruling even though its cause exists. Here, the husband's right prevents the umm walad's 'iddah from the master from taking effect while he is alive.
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat al-shubha): A doubt within a doubt; a probability that is too weak to be considered legally. Used here to argue that the possibility of obligation of 'iddah from the master is not established.
احتياط (ihtiyat): Precaution; applying a ruling when there is doubt, to ensure that a potential obligation is not missed. Here, the Sahibayn use it to justify combining 'iddahs.

D) Because the umm walad is free

Key Takeaway
When the cause is certain and the preventive factor is in doubt, Hanafi precaution may require applying the ruling, as argued by the Sahibayn for the umm walad's combined 'iddahs.

Wife of a Minor: 'Iddah for a Pregnant Widow
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah of a minor's pregnant widow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the 'iddah of a minor's pregnant widow and identify the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
A minor cannot father a child, so pregnancy only shortens the 'iddah if birth occurs within six months of his death.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the 'iddah of a wife whose husband, a minor, dies while she is pregnant. The core question: does the pregnancy change the widow's 'iddah from the standard four months and ten days? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, but only if birth occurs within six months; Abu Yusuf says no, retaining the full widow's 'iddah in all cases. Understanding the reasoning behind each view sharpens our grasp of how lineage (nasab) and legal capacity affect family rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the case: a minor husband dies, leaving a pregnant wife. Determine the default 'iddah for a widow (four months and ten days).
The minor's marriage is valid, but he is not legally capable of fathering children.
2
Identify the two positions
Distinguish the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the child's birth within six months ends the 'iddah; otherwise the full widow's 'iddah applies) from Abu Yusuf's view (always the full widow's 'iddah).
Both agree that 'iddah is required; the dispute is about its duration.
3
Analyze the reasoning (qiyas)
Both sides base their argument on the impossibility of paternity for a minor. Abu Yusuf reasons that since the pregnancy cannot be attributed to the deceased, it is irrelevant—the widow's 'iddah is fixed. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that if the birth occurs within the shortest possible pregnancy (six months) from death, delivery itself terminates the 'iddah as per the general rule. If later, the pregnancy is unrelated to the marriage, so the standard widow's 'iddah applies.
The six-month threshold is the minimum human pregnancy period acknowledged in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a concrete example: if the minor dies and his wife gives birth four months later, under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad her 'iddah ends at delivery; under Abu Yusuf she must wait four months and ten days from death regardless.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the widow can remarry earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وزوجة الصغير الحامل عند موته وضعه والحامل بعدة الشهور) أي عدتها وضع الحمل إذا أتت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت موته وعدتها الشهور إذا أتت به لستة أشهر فأكثر أي عدة الوفاة أربعة أشهر وعشر والحامل صفة زوجة وهو نعت مخصوص بالإناث كحائض ولهذا لم يؤنث. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وأوجب أبو يوسف عدة الوفاة في الحالين لأن الحمل ليس ثابت النسب منه فاستوى الموجود عند الموت والحادث بعده. ولهما
His saying: 'And the wife of the minor who is pregnant at his death: her delivery (is the 'iddah) and the pregnant one (is subject to) 'iddah of months.' Meaning her 'iddah is the delivery of the pregnancy if she gives birth to it at less than six months from the time of his death, and her 'iddah is months if she gives birth to it at six months or more, i.e., the 'iddah of death: four months and ten days. The word 'pregnant' is a description of wife, and it is a masculine adjective specific to females like 'ha'id' (menstruating), therefore it is not feminized. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf made the 'iddah of death obligatory in both cases, because the pregnancy is not established as his lineage, so the one present at death and the one occurring after are equal. And for them both...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the 'iddah of a wife whose husband is a minor (saghir) and dies while she is pregnant. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if she gives birth within six months of the husband's death, the 'iddah is concluded by delivery (putting down the pregnancy); if the birth occurs at six months or more, she must observe the full 'iddah of widowhood (four months and ten days) because the child's lineage cannot be attributed to the minor husband (since a minor cannot father a child). Abu Yusuf holds that in both cases she must observe the 'iddah of death (four months and ten days) because the pregnancy is not legally proven to be from him, so the existence of pregnancy at his death does not affect the 'iddah. The text also clarifies grammatical usage: 'al-hamil' (pregnant) is an adjective describing the wife, using masculine form because it is a specific pattern for female adjectives like 'ha'id'. The text cuts off before presenting the Sahibayn's proof, but the abbreviation 'wa lahuma' indicates that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (both Sahibayn) hold this view, though here it seems Abu Yusuf differs from Muhammad. Actually the text says 'عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد' so Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa, and Abu Yusuf alone differs. The passage ends with 'ولهما' suggesting the Sahibayn have a proof, but it is missing.

Key Terms
زوجة الصغير (zawjat al-saghir): The wife of a minor (a boy who has not reached puberty). The marriage of a minor can be contracted by the guardian, but the husband's legal capacity is limited.
وضع الحمل (wad' al-haml): Delivery of the pregnancy; the act of giving birth. In 'iddah, it is a way of concluding the waiting period if the birth occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Delivery ends the 'iddah.
B) Four months and ten days.
C) Three months.
D) No 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the 'iddah of a minor's pregnant widow depends on whether birth occurs within six months of his death: if yes, delivery ends the 'iddah; if no, the full widow's 'iddah applies; Abu Yusuf holds the latter in all cases.

Applying Ihtiyat (Precaution) in 'Iddah for Unknown Sequence of Deaths
Explaining the ruling on 'iddah when sequence of deaths is unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ihtiyat to determine whether 'iddah is required when there is doubt about an impediment versus doubt about the cause.

Core Idea
When the cause is certain but the impediment is doubtful, precaution imposes the obligation; ihtiyat does not apply when the cause itself is doubtful.

In cases where the sequence of deaths is unknown, the Hanafi school uses the principle of ihtiyat (precaution) to ensure that a woman does not miss a required 'iddah. This lesson unpacks the ruling using qiyas and a consensus-based argument, clarifying the crucial distinction between doubt about the cause and doubt about an impediment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Cause and Impediment
First, recognize that the death of the master is the cause (sabab) for 'iddah with three menstruations (for a slave woman). The husband's prior right (marriage) acts as an impediment (mani'). Determine whether the doubt concerns the existence of the cause or the persistence of the impediment.
2
Apply the Principle of Ihtiyat
If the cause is certain (death occurred) but there is uncertainty about whether the impediment (husband's right) still existed at that time, precaution dictates that the 'iddah is obligatory. The ruling is attached to the stronger probability.
3
Analyze the Analogy (Qiyas)
Understand the analogy: a man who marries multiple women in one contract and dies without specifying the order—all must observe 'iddah. The cause (death) is certain; the impediment (which marriage is valid) is doubtful. Compare this to the case of unknown death sequence.
4
Recognize the Consensus on 'Doubt of a Doubt'
The text states that a 'doubt of a doubt' (shubhat al-shubha) is disregarded by consensus. That is, if there is doubt about whether the impediment actually existed, and that doubt is based on another doubt (e.g., whether the master died before or after two months), it is not considered.
5
Distinguish Doubt about Cause vs. Impediment
If the doubt is about the cause itself (e.g., whether a condition for divorce occurred), ihtiyat does not apply because the ruling cannot be established without a certain cause. Thus, in such cases, no 'iddah is required.

التقديرين دون الآخر فكان الاحتمال الثابت قطعاً قائماً الحقيقة عملاً بالاحتياط، ولا يقام احتمال وجوب العدة عن المولى لأن شبهة الشبهة ساقطة الاعتبار بالإجماع بخلاف وجوب العدة على أولئك النساء لثبوت احتمال وجوب العدة عليهن لأن نكاح كل فريق إما أن يكون متقدماً أو لم يكن، فإن تقدم وجبت العدة قطعاً وإلا لا تجب قطعاً . فيكون الاحتمال ثابتاً فيلحق بالحقيقة .اهـ. وقال في فتح القدير بعد الدليلين: ولا يخفى أنه مشترك الإلزام. وفي الكافي للحاكم الشهيد إن قولهما احتياط. وفي فتح القدير : إن الاحتياط إنما يكون بعد ظهور السبب لأنه العمل بأقوى الدليلين. ثم قال في الكافي : ولا ميراث لها من زوجها لأني لم أعلم أنها كانت حرة يوم موته اهـ. وفيه: ولا فرق بين كون طلاقها رجعياً أو بائناً في الوجوه كلها وفيه أيضاً لو مات عن أم ولده أو أعتقها فجاءت بولد ما بينها وبين سنتين لزمه، وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين لم يلزمه إلا أن فإن ادعاه لزمه اهـ. وفي الخانية : أم ولد أعتقها مولاها أو مات ولزمتها العدة ثم تزوجت في العدة فجاءت بولد لسنتين من حين مات المولى أو أعتق ولستة أشهر منذ تزوجت وادعياه معاً كان للمولى في قولهم لمكان العدة التي كانت . يدعيه
The death, and it is permissible that the husband died first and two months and five days passed, then the master died, so three menstruations become obligatory. This is because the death of the master is a cause for observing 'iddah by three menstruations, and the right of the husband is an impediment, and since the impediment remains, the ruling of the cause becomes obligatory as a precaution for her, just as if he married two daughters in one contract and three in a contract and four in a contract and died unknown, then 'iddah is obligatory on all because of the existence of the cause and the doubt about the impediment regarding distinguishing, which is the precedence of one party's marriage over another. Unlike if the doubt is about the cause, then no precaution is taken to establish the ruling because it is impossible to establish the ruling without the cause, as if he said, 'If I do not do such and such, you are divorced,' then he dies, and it is not known whether the condition occurred or not, then she does not observe 'iddah of divorce because of the doubt about the cause, since it is effective only when the condition exists, and its existence is doubtful. And he said that reality is only for probability, except that one of the two probabilities is certain and the other is probable. Explanation: The death of the husband after the master necessarily requires observing the 'iddah of death, and this probability is certain. The probability of the husband's death before the master's death does not necessarily require observing 'iddah by three menstruations, because it is possible that the master's death after the husband occurs before the expiry of two months and five days, so it is not obligatory, and it is possible that it occurs after the expiry of this period, so it is obligatory. Thus the probability is certain in one of two possibilities but not the other. So the probability that is certain is considered as reality in operation, acting on precaution. And the probability of obligation of 'iddah from the master is not considered because a doubt of a doubt is disregarded by consensus, unlike the obligation of 'iddah upon those women because the probability of obligation of 'iddah upon them is established, since the marriage of each party is either earlier or not. If it is earlier, 'iddah is obligatory definitely; otherwise, it is not obligatory definitely. Thus the probability is established, so it is attached to reality. End. And in Fath al-Qadir after the two proofs: it is not hidden that it is common in incumbency. In al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid: indeed their saying is precaution. In Fath al-Qadir: precaution is only after the appearance of the cause because it is acting on the stronger of two proofs. Then he said in al-Kafi: And she has no inheritance from her husband because I do not know that she was free on the day of his death. End. And in it: there is no difference between her divorce being revocable or irrevocable in all aspects. And in it also: if he dies leaving an umm walad, or frees her, and she gives birth to a child within two years, it is attributed to him; if she gives birth after more than two years, it is not attributed to him unless he claims it, then it is attributed to him. End. And in al-Khaniyah: an umm walad whom her master freed or died, and 'iddah became obligatory upon her, then she married during the 'iddah, and she gave birth two years after the master's death or freeing, and six months since she married, and both claimed the child, it is for the master according to their saying because of the 'iddah that was. He claims.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting a ruling on 'iddah when there is doubt about the sequence of deaths. The specific case involves a woman who is married to both a husband and a master? Actually the text mentions 'al-zawj' (husband) and 'al-mawla' (master). The scenario is that the husband died first, and after two months and five days, the master dies. The ruling is that she must observe 'iddah by three menstruations. The reasoning is that the master's death is the cause for 'iddah of three menstruations (for a slave woman), but the husband's right is an impediment. Since the impediment remains (the husband died first, the marriage ended), the cause becomes effective. However, we are uncertain whether the husband died before or after the master? Actually the text initially assumes the husband died first, then extends to a case of doubt. The main point is: when the cause (death) is certain but there is doubt about whether an impediment existed, the ruling is imposed as a precaution (ihtiyat). The text gives an analogy: a man marries several women in one contract (e.g., two daughters, three, four) and dies without specifying which marriage was first. All women must observe 'iddah because the cause (death) is certain and the impediment (which marriage is valid) is doubtful. This is contrasted with doubt about the cause itself, where no precaution is taken. The passage then provides a detailed rational analysis: the probability that the husband died after the master is certain in one scenario (if the master died before the husband and after two months five days have passed), so that probability is treated as reality out of precaution. The text also notes that a 'doubt of a doubt' is disregarded by consensus. Then quotes from Fath al-Qadir and al-Kafi are presented, including additional rulings: no inheritance for the woman if her free status at death is unknown; no difference between revocable and irrevocable divorce; and a ruling about an umm walad (slave mother) who is freed or whose master dies, and she remarries during 'iddah: if she gives birth within two years and six months and both claim the child, it belongs to the master because of the 'iddah. This is a complex set of rulings, but the central theme is the application of ihtiyat in 'iddah cases.

Key Terms
احتياط (ihtiyat): A principle of precaution in Hanafi fiqh where, when there is doubt about legal obligations, the ruling that is more cautious (e.g., imposing a potential obligatory act) is preferred.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce, during which she cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling for unknown sequence of deaths (husband and master), what is the key distinction that determines whether ihtiyat applies?
A) Whether the woman is free or slave.
B) Whether the doubt is about the cause or about the impediment.
C) Whether the husband died before or after the master.
D) Whether the divorce was revocable or irrevocable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of ihtiyat requires imposing 'iddah when the cause is certain but the impediment is doubtful, using qiyas and disregarding a 'doubt of a doubt' by consensus.

Talaq — Divorce

The 'Iddah of a Minor's Pregnant Wife: Dispute Between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah of minor's wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the 'iddah of a minor's pregnant wife, explain the proof of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
For a minor's pregnant wife, 'iddah ends with delivery only if she gives birth within six months; otherwise, it is by months.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement: what is the 'iddah of a woman whose minor husband dies while she is pregnant? The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule according to the timing of birth relative to death, while Abu Yusuf holds a uniform rule. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle cases where pregnancy and death intersect, and highlights the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between the wife giving birth within six months of the husband's death and giving birth after six months. This timing determines whether the pregnancy is considered 'existing' (mawjud) or 'subsequent' (hadith).
This is the key factual distinction that drives the ruling.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if birth occurs within six months, the 'iddah ends with delivery. If birth occurs after six months, the 'iddah is the full four months and ten days (by months).
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing position
Abu Yusuf holds that in both scenarios, the 'iddah is by months (four months and ten days), regardless of delivery. He argues that the pregnancy is not established in lineage from the minor, so both cases are equal.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the proof of the majority
The majority cites Quran 65:4 ('And those among you who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden') and reasons that the purpose of 'iddah for a pregnant woman is to fulfill the right of marriage, which applies even if the child is not from the minor. This purpose is served only for the existing pregnancy; a subsequent pregnancy does not alter the already-fixed months-based 'iddah.
Note the distinction: the general verse applies only when the pregnancy existed at death.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference emerges when the wife gives birth before completing four months ten days but after more than six months? Actually, if she gives birth within six months, the majority ends her 'iddah at delivery (which could be before four months ten days), while Abu Yusuf requires her to complete four months ten days even if she delivers earlier. If she gives birth after six months, both agree on months, but the majority views her 'iddah as already completed at four months ten days (since that is before six months), so she can remarry before delivery.
Thus, the difference affects when she can remarry.

His saying: (And the pregnant wife of the minor at his death, her delivery, and the pregnant one by months) i.e. her 'iddah is delivery if she gives birth less than six months from the time of his death, and her 'iddah is months if she gives birth at six months or more, i.e. the 'iddah of death is four months and ten days. 'Pregnant' is an attribute of wife, and it is a specific adjective for females like 'menstruating', thus it is not feminized. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf made the 'iddah of death obligatory in both cases because the pregnancy is not established in lineage from him, so the existing and the subsequent are equal. In their view, the absolute wording of the Almighty: 'And those among you who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden' [At-Talaq:4], and because it is determined by the duration of giving birth for pregnant women, whether the period is short or long, not for ascertaining the emptiness of the womb because it is legislated by months and the existence of menstrual periods, but to fulfill the right of marriage. Therefore we qualified it by her giving birth less than two years. Likewise if she marries during the 'iddah of death and becomes pregnant, but there is a contrary opinion in contrast to the 'iddah of divorce. Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the matn statement: 'the pregnant wife of a minor at his death, her delivery and the pregnant one by months.' The commentator clarifies that if the wife of a minor boy is pregnant at the time of his death, her 'iddah is by delivery if she gives birth within six months of his death, indicating that the pregnancy existed at death. If she gives birth after six months, the pregnancy is considered new, so her 'iddah is by months (four months ten days). This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that she must observe the 'iddah of death (four months ten days) in both cases, regardless of when she gives birth, because the pregnancy is not established as being from the minor (since he is a minor, lineage is not proved easily), so both situations are equal. The text provides the proof of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): they cite the Quranic verse 'And those among you who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden' (At-Talaq:4) which generally makes the 'iddah of a pregnant woman end with delivery. However, they distinguish: if the pregnancy existed at the time of death (i.e., she gives birth within six months), then her 'iddah is by delivery because the purpose of 'iddah is to fulfill the right of marriage (to prevent mixing of lineages), and that purpose exists even though the child is not from the minor. If the pregnancy occurs after death (she gives birth after six months), then the 'iddah was already determined by months and does not change due to a new pregnancy. The text then discusses the interpretation of 'existing' and 'subsequent' pregnancy according to al-Hidayah and other sources. It also expands on the status of the minor's intercourse: whether valid or invalid marriage, and whether seclusion (khalwa) or actual intercourse triggers 'iddah. The passage includes quotes from al-Qunyah, Ahad al-Jurjani, and others, showing the details: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say both dower and 'iddah are obligatory from intercourse with a minor if he is near puberty, while Muhammad says only 'iddah, not dower, because the minor's intercourse is legally considered like his finger (not fully productive). The discussion then moves to the case of an adult man whose wife becomes pregnant after his death: according to al-Hidayah, if the pregnancy occurs after death, she does not observe 'iddah by delivery but by months, because lineage is established from him (since he is an adult), so the pregnancy is considered existing at death legally. The passage ends with additional rulings from al-Mujtaba and al-Idah about pregnant women in various 'iddah situations. Finally, a quote from Kafi al-Hakim about the insane husband begins but is cut off.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce, which varies depending on the status of pregnancy and the husband's age.
صغير (saghir): A minor male who has not reached puberty; his marriage is valid but special rules apply for intercourse and 'iddah.
موجود وحادث (mawjud wa hadith): Existing pregnancy (mawjud) is one evidenced by birth within 6 months of death; subsequent (hadith) is birth after 6 months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Her 'iddah ends with delivery regardless.

C) Her 'iddah is the same as a non-pregnant woman, i.e., three menstrual cycles.
D) She has no 'iddah because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
For a minor's wife, the 'iddah ends with delivery only if she gives birth within six months of his death; otherwise, it is four months and ten days.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The iddah of a Pregnant Widow: Existing vs. New Pregnancy
Explaining the Qur'anic basis and Hanafi definition of mawjud and hadith pregnancy in iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling that a pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery and DISTINGUISH between mawjud and hadith pregnancy based on the time of birth relative to the husband's death.

Core Idea
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, but the classification of pregnancy as mawjud or hadith affects when that delivery occurs.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Qur'anic basis for the iddah of a pregnant widow. The Hanafi school holds that the iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery, whether the pregnancy existed at death or began after. However, a key distinction is made: if the pregnancy is new (hadith), the iddah was already established by months and does not change upon pregnancy. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the iddah of a widow who is pregnant. The ruling is that her iddah ends with delivery (wajib).
This is established by the Quranic verse.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites Quran 65:4: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden.' This is the primary evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'ajaluhunna an yada'na hamlahunna' indicates that delivery terminates the iddah. The Hanafis take this as a definitive (qat'i) specification of the term.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on definitions
Within the Hanafi school, there is disagreement on how to define mawjud (existing) and hadith (new) pregnancy. The preferred view is that hadith is birth within six months of death, and mawjud is birth in less than six months.
Another view defines hadith as birth after more than two years, but the first view is more correct.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that a pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery in all cases, but the classification affects the reasoning: for existing pregnancy, the iddah is by delivery; for new pregnancy, the iddah was already by months and delivery is not relevant.

كتاب الطلاق باب العدة كذا من أن إطلاق قوله تعالى وأولات الاحمال أجلهن أن يضعن حملهن ﴾ [الطلاق: ٤] ولأنها مقدرة بمدة وضع الحمل في أولات الأحمال قصرت المدة أو طالت لا للتعرف عن فراغ الرحم لشرعها بالأشهر وجود الأقراء لكن لقضاء حق النكاح وهذا المعنى يتحقق في حق مع الصبي وإن لم يكن الحمل منه بخلاف الحمل الحادث لأنه وجبت العدة بالشهور فلا يتغير بحدوث الحمل الحادث بعده وفيما نحن فيه كما وجبت وجبت مقدرة بمدة الحمل فافترقا ؛ في الهداية واختلفوا في الموجود والحادث فالصحيح في تفسيرهما ما قدمناه الحادث أن تأتي به بعد موته لستة أشهر من يوم الموت وهو قول عامة المشايخ. وقال : أن تضعه لأكثر من سنتين والأول أصح؛ كذا في العناية معزياً إلى النهاية. وأما بعضهم : تفسير قيامه عند الموت أن تلده لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الموت؛ كذا في الفوائد الظهيرية .
Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. ... from that the general application of His statement, the Most High: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden' [Quran 65:4]. And because it is measured by the duration of giving birth for pregnant women, whether the period is short or long, not for ascertaining the emptiness of the womb because it is legislated by months and the existence of menstruations, but for fulfilling the right of marriage. And this meaning is realized with respect to the minor even if the pregnancy is not from him, contrary to the new pregnancy because the 'iddah was established by months, so it does not change by the occurrence of a new pregnancy after it. And in what we are discussing, just as it is established, it is established measured by the duration of pregnancy, so they are different. In al-Hidayah: They differed regarding the existing and the new. The correct interpretation of them is what we have presented: the new is that she gives birth after his death within six months from the day of death, and this is the saying of the majority of the mashayikh. And he said: that she gives birth after more than two years, and the first is more correct. Thus in al-'Inayah attributed to al-Nihayah. And some of them: the interpretation of its existence at the time of death is that she gives birth in less than six months from the time of death; thus in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a sharh explaining the ruling on the iddah of a pregnant widow. The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Talaq (65:4) which establishes that the iddah for pregnant women ends with delivery. The Hanafi position is that this applies whether the pregnancy existed at the time of death (mawjud) or occurred after death (hadith), but with a crucial distinction. For a woman who is pregnant at the time of her husband's death, her iddah is by delivery regardless of the duration. This is because the purpose of iddah here is not merely to ascertain the emptiness of the womb but rather to fulfill the right of the marriage contract, which is fulfilled by the birth. For a woman who becomes pregnant after her husband's death, the iddah was initially established by months at the time of death, and the subsequent pregnancy does not change it. The text then discusses the definitions of 'existing' (mawjud) and 'new' (hadith) pregnancy. The correct view is that 'new' means she gives birth within six months of death, and 'existing' means she gives birth within less than six months. Another view defines existing as birth within less than six months. The discussion also notes that the iddah of a pregnant widow is by delivery even if the pregnancy is from zina, as will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الموجود (al-mawjud): Pregnancy existing at the time of the husband's death, defined as birth in less than six months from death.
الحادث (al-hadith): Pregnancy occurring after the husband's death, defined as birth within six months of death according to the preferred view.
العِدَّة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the 'iddah of a woman whose husband dies and she is pregnant at the time of his death?
A) Four months and ten days
B) The delivery of the child
C) Three menstrual cycles
D) Six months after death

Key Takeaway
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, but the classification of pregnancy as existing (mawjud) or new (hadith) depends on the timing of birth relative to the husband's death.

The Mahr Dispute When a Minor Husband Has Intercourse
Ruling with proof on the mahr obligation for a minor husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on mahr for a minor husband and explain the reconciliation by distinguishing between a murahiq and a younger child.

Core Idea
A minor husband's intercourse always imposes iddah, but mahr depends on whether he can produce sperm.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a minor boy marries a woman and has intercourse. Does the wife observe iddah? Is the mahr obligatory? The Hanafi school agrees on iddah but differs on mahr. The key is the minor's age and physical capacity. Let's explore the positions and their reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intercourse by a minor husband. The ruling agreed upon is that iddah is wajib for the wife.
2
Locate the dispute
The dispute is on the obligation of mahr (dower). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say mahr is wajib; Muhammad says it is not.
3
Trace the basis of the disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider a minor who is murahiq (adolescent) from whom sperm is possible. Muhammad considers a very young child who cannot produce sperm; his intercourse is like inserting a finger.
4
Understand the reconciliation
The disagreement is not contradictory but applies to different scenarios. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf speak of a murahiq, Muhammad speaks of a younger child. Thus, both positions are valid in their contexts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is to differentiate: for a murahiq, mahr is wajib; for a younger child, only iddah is wajib without mahr.
This reconciliation is supported by al-Qunyah and other sources.

Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage covers several special cases concerning iddah. First, the author addresses the situation of a minor husband. The key point is that if a minor (who has not reached puberty) marries a woman and has intercourse, either in a valid or invalid marriage, the wife must observe iddah. This is because the iddah is due to the act of intercourse, not necessarily the capacity to produce offspring. However, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other regarding the obligation of mahr (dower). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say both mahr and iddah are wajib, while Muhammad says only iddah is wajib. The reconciliation is that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf are speaking about a minor who is murahiq (adolescent) and can potentially produce sperm, while Muhammad speaks of a younger child who cannot. For such a child, intercourse is like inserting a finger, so no mahr is due, but iddah is still wajib because iddah is based on the act of insertion, not on the possibility of pregnancy. The text then moves to a discussion of a woman who is pregnant from zina and later marries. According to the Hanafi school, if she marries while pregnant from zina, and her husband dies or divorces her, her iddah ends with delivery. This is because the delivery of the child, even if from zina, fulfills the purpose of iddah. However, there is a nuance: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) hold that if the husband did not know of the pregnancy, the marriage is valid and iddah by delivery, but if he knew, the marriage is not valid. The eunuch (khasi) and the impotent man (majbub) are also discussed. A eunuch is like a normal man regarding iddah and lineage. An impotent man who can still ejaculate is like a normal man; if he cannot, he is like a minor regarding lineage but iddah still applies? The text says: 'The eunuch is like the sound man in lineage and iddah, likewise the man with severed genitals [majbub] if he ejaculates; if he does not ejaculate, the child does not belong to him, so he is like the minor regarding lineage and iddah.' Finally, the passage notes that a menstrual period in which divorce occurs is not counted in the iddah, because counting it would cause a reduction in the prescribed iddah (which is three full menstrual cycles). This is a ruling upon which there is consensus among the Hanafis, differing from Malik and Shafi'i who count that period if the divorce occurred during a state of purity.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor male who has not reached puberty.
المراهق (al-murahiq): An adolescent minor who is close to puberty and from whom sperm is possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on mahr for a wife who has intercourse with her minor husband who is very young and cannot produce sperm?
A) Mahr is wajib
B) Mahr is not wajib
C) Mahr is wajib only if divorce occurs
D) Mahr is wajib only if pregnancy occurs

Key Takeaway
Iddah is always wajib by a minor husband's entry; mahr is wajib only if he can produce sperm (murahiq).

intermediate

Iddah for Pregnancy from Zina and for Eunuchs/Impotent Men
Ruling on iddah for special cases: pregnancy from zina and eunuch/impotent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah by delivery for a woman who was pregnant from zina and the rulings for eunuchs and impotent men regarding lineage and iddah.

Core Idea
A woman pregnant from zina ends her iddah by delivery; eunuchs and impotent men who can ejaculate are like normal men for iddah.

This passage continues with special cases: a woman marries while pregnant from zina, then her husband divorces or dies. Her iddah is by delivery, not by months, according to the majority of Hanafi imams. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Additionally, the passage covers eunuchs and men with severed genitals (majbub): if they can still ejaculate, they are treated as normal for lineage and iddah; if not, they are like minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
Two scenarios: (1) a woman pregnant from zina who marries another man, then divorced or widowed; (2) a eunuch or majbub husband.
2
State the ruling for pregnancy from zina
The majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say her iddah is by delivery. Abu Hanifa says by months if unknown.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if the husband did not know of the pregnancy, the marriage is valid in effect and iddah by delivery. Abu Hanifa says iddah by three months (or by delivery? Actually the text says 'according to them contrary to him' meaning Abu Hanifa says months).
The text says: 'she observes iddah by delivery according to them, contrary to him (Abu Hanifa).'
4
Apply to eunuch and majbub
A eunuch (khasi) is like a normal man in lineage and iddah. A majbub (severed penis) who can ejaculate is like a normal man; if he cannot ejaculate, no lineage to him, and he is like a minor for iddah (still wajib but lineage not established).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for pregnancy from zina is iddah by delivery (the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For eunuch/majbub, the ruling depends on ability to ejaculate.

ولم أر صريحاً حكم دخول الصبي في النكاح الصحيح والفاسد في وجوب العدة وقد صرحوا بفساد خلوته وبوجوب العدة بالخلوة الفاسدة الشاملة لخلوة الصبي، وإنما الكلام فيما إذا أولج فيها في مكان ليس بخلوة هل تجب به العدة لو بلغ وطلقها. ثم رأيت في شرح النكاح الفاسد من هذا الكتاب أني نقلت وجوب العدة عليها إذا وطئها الصبي بنكاح فاسد وفي وجوب المهر عليه بالوطء تفصيل فليرجع إليه . فعلم به أن دخوله في الصحيح موجب للعدة عليها بالأولى وخلوته كدخوله فيها فحاصله أن الزوج الصبي كالبالغ في الصحيح والفاسد وفي الوطء بشبهة في الوفاة والطلاق والتفريق ووضع الحمل كما ل يخفى فليحفظ . ثم رأيت في القنية ما نصه : تجب العدة بدخول زوجها الصبي المراهق وفي آحاد الجرجاني في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف أن المهر والعدة واجبان بوطء الصبي، وفي قول محمد تجب العدة دون المهر ثم قال : ولا خلاف بينهم لأنهما أجابا في مراهق يتصور منه الإعلاق ومحمد أجاب فيمن لا يتصور منه الإعلاق لأن ذكره في حكم أصبعه. وفي نظم الزندوستي : زنت العاقلة البالغة بصبي أو مجنون لأحد عليهما وعليها العدة ولا مهر لها اهـ. المسألة الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي فيما إذا كان رضيعاً.

وضعه والحامل بعدة الشهور والنسب منتف فيهما ولم تعتد بحيض طلقت فيه وتجب يصح كان حكمه في العدة والولد حكم الرجل الصحيح وفي الخانية قبيل المهر : زوج أمته من رضيع ثم جاءت بولد قادعاه المولى ثبت نسبه لأنه أقر بنسب من يملكه وليس له نسب معروف، ولو كان الزوج مجبوباً لم يثبت النسب من المولى لأنه ثابت النسب من الزوج وعلى الزوج كل المهر لمكان الدخول حكماً .اهـ. والحق أن قول أبي يوسف موافق لقولهما وإنما هي رواية شاذة عنه موافقة للشافعي وهو رواية عن الإمام أيضاً كما حققه في فتح القدير وفيه : وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا طلق الكبير امرأته فأتت بولد غير سقط لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت العقد بأن تزوجها حاملاً. من الزنا ولا يعلم الحال وإنما وضعت كذلك بعد الطلاق تعتد بالوضع عندهما خلافاً له. وإنما قلنا ولا يعلم ليصح كونه على هذا الخلاف لأنه لو علم لا العقد عند أبي يوسف لأنه يمنع العقد على الحبلى من الزنا بخلاف ما إذا لم يعلم فإنه وإن لم يصححه لكن يوجب من الوطء فيه العدة لأنه شبهة فيقع الخلاف في أنها بالوضع أو بالأشهر اهـ. وفي البدائع : وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد في زوجة الكبير تأتي بولد بعد موته لأكثر من سنتين وقد تزوجت بعد مضي أربعة أشهر وعشر إن النكاح جائز لأن إقدامها على النكاح إقرار منها بالانقضاء ولم يرد ما يبطل ذلك قوله : والنسب منتف فيهما) أي في الموجود وقت الموت والحادث بعده لأن الصبي لا ماء له فلا يتصور منه العلوق، ولا يرد ثبوت نسب ولد امرأة المشرقي من المغربية لأن النكاح إنما أقمناه مقام العلوق لتصوره حقيقة وهو غير متصور هنا حقيقة فافترقا وظاهر إطلاقهم دخول المراهق وينبغي أن يثبت النسب احتياطاً إلا أن لا يمكن بأن جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت العقد كما في فتح القدير، ولهذا المسألة صور الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي بما إذا كان رضيعاً ودل كلامهم في زوجة الصغير أن الحامل من الزنا إذا تزوجت ثم مات عنها زوجها فعدتها بوضع الحمل كما صرح به في المعراج معزياً إلى قاضيخان وقدمنا أن الحامل من الزنا لا عدة عليها عندهما ولذا صححا نكاحها لغير الزاني وإن حرما الوطء وإنما الكلام فيما إذا تزوجت على قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وهي حامل من الزنا ثم طلقها أو مات عنها فإنها تعتد بوضع الحمل. وفي كافي الحاكم الشهيد في عدة امرأة الصغير إذا مات وهي حامل فإن عدتها بوضع الحمل قال لأنه مات وهي حامل وإن كان من فجور والخصي كالصحيح في الولد والعدة وكذلك المجبوب إذا كان ينزل وإن لم ينزل لم يلزمه الولد فكان بمنزلة الصبي في الولد والعدة قوله : (ولم تعتد بحيض طلقت فيه للزوم النقص عن المقدر شرعاً لو اعتد بها وهذا بالإجماع بخلاف الطهر الذي وقع فيه الطلاق فإنه محسوب عند مالك والشافعي، وقد أورد عليهما لزوم قوله : (والحق أن قول أبي يوسف الخ) راجع لمسألة المتن. النقصان عن
And I have not seen explicitly the ruling of the entry of a minor into valid and invalid marriage regarding the obligation of iddah. And they have stated the invalidity of his khalwah and the obligation of iddah by invalid khalwah which includes the khalwah of a minor. Rather the discussion is if he inserted in a place that is not khalwah, does iddah become obligatory if he reaches puberty and divorces her? Then I saw in the commentary on invalid marriage from this book that I transmitted the obligation of iddah upon her if the minor had intercourse with her in an invalid marriage, and regarding the obligation of mahr upon him by intercourse there is detail, so refer to it. So it is understood that his entry in a valid marriage necessitates iddah upon her a fortiori, and his khalwah is like his entry. So the conclusion is that the minor husband is like an adult in valid and invalid marriage, and in intercourse by shubha in death, divorce, separation, and delivery of pregnancy, as is not hidden, so memorize. Then I saw in al-Qunyah: text: 'Iddah becomes obligatory by the entry of her husband the adolescent minor. And in Ahad al-Jurjani, in the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, that mahr and iddah are obligatory by the intercourse of the minor. In the saying of Muhammad, iddah is obligatory without mahr.' Then he said: There is no disagreement among them because they answered regarding an adolescent from whom conception is possible, and Muhammad answered regarding one from whom conception is not possible because his penis is like his finger. And in Nazm al-Zandustuti: 'The sane adult woman who commits zina with a minor or an insane person: upon both of them, and upon her is iddah, and she has no mahr.' End. The matter: al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi: in the case when he is a suckling infant. ... its delivery and the pregnant woman’s iddah by months, and lineage is negated in both, and she does not observe iddah by a menstruation in which divorce occurred ... and it is necessary ... his ruling in iddah and child is the ruling of a sound man. And in al-Khaniyyah before the section on mahr: 'He married his slave girl to a suckling infant, then she came with a child, and both claimed it: the master's lineage is established because he acknowledged lineage of one he owns and he has no known lineage. And if the husband was a man with severed genitals, lineage from the master is not established because it is established from the husband, and upon the husband is the entire mahr due to the entry virtually.' End. And the truth is that the saying of Abu Yusuf agrees with their saying, and it is a rare narration from him agreeing with al-Shafi'i, and it is a narration from the Imam also as verified in Fath al-Qadir. And it includes: Upon this ikhtilaf, if an adult divorces his wife and she gives birth to a non-miscarried child less than six months from the time of the contract, meaning he married her while she was pregnant from zina and the situation is unknown, and she gave birth after divorce, she observes iddah by delivery according to them, contrary to him. And we said 'and it is unknown' to make it correct that it is upon this ikhtilaf, because if he knew, the contract would be invalid according to Abu Yusuf because he prohibits marriage to a pregnant woman from zina, contrary to when he does not know: then even if he does not validate it, it necessitates iddah from intercourse because it is shubha, so the ikhtilaf occurs whether it is by delivery or by months. End. And in al-Bada'i': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said regarding the wife of an adult who gives birth after his death after more than two years, and she married after passing of four months and ten days: the marriage is permissible because her proceeding to marriage is an admission from her of the expiration, and nothing has come to invalidate that. His statement: 'and lineage is negated in both' i.e., in the existing at the time of death and the new after it, because the minor has no fluid so conception cannot be imagined from him. And the establishment of lineage of the child of the woman of the Easterner from the Westerner is not refuted because we established marriage in place of conception because it is conceivable in reality, and it is not conceivable here in reality, so they differ. And the apparent import of their statements includes the adolescent, and it is fitting that lineage be established as a precaution unless it is impossible, as when she gives birth less than six months from the time of the contract, as in Fath al-Qadir. And for this case, al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi gave examples including when he is a suckling infant. And their statements indicate regarding the wife of a minor that a pregnant woman from zina, if she marries and then her husband dies, her iddah is by delivery of the child, as is stated in al-Mi'raj attributed to Qadikhan. And we have stated that the pregnant woman from zina has no iddah according to them, so they validate her marriage to other than the fornicator, even if they prohibit intercourse. Rather the discussion is regarding when she marries according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad while she is pregnant from zina, then he divorces her or dies, then she observes iddah by delivery. And in al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid regarding the iddah of the wife of a minor when she dies while pregnant, her iddah is by delivery of the child, he said: because she died while pregnant even if from fornication. And the eunuch is like the sound man in lineage and iddah, likewise the man with severed genitals if he ejaculates; if he does not ejaculate, the child does not belong to him, so he is like the minor in lineage and iddah. His statement: 'and she does not observe iddah by a menstruation in which divorce occurred' because of the necessary reduction from what is legally prescribed if she were to count it, and this is by consensus, contrary to the pure period in which divorce occurred, for it is counted according to Malik and al-Shafi'i, and it has been objected to them regarding the necessity of...
