Commentary
This passage covers several special cases concerning iddah. First, the author addresses the situation of a minor husband. The key point is that if a minor (who has not reached puberty) marries a woman and has intercourse, either in a valid or invalid marriage, the wife must observe iddah. This is because the iddah is due to the act of intercourse, not necessarily the capacity to produce offspring. However, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other regarding the obligation of mahr (dower). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say both mahr and iddah are wajib, while Muhammad says only iddah is wajib. The reconciliation is that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf are speaking about a minor who is murahiq (adolescent) and can potentially produce sperm, while Muhammad speaks of a younger child who cannot. For such a child, intercourse is like inserting a finger, so no mahr is due, but iddah is still wajib because iddah is based on the act of insertion, not on the possibility of pregnancy. The text then moves to a discussion of a woman who is pregnant from zina and later marries. According to the Hanafi school, if she marries while pregnant from zina, and her husband dies or divorces her, her iddah ends with delivery. This is because the delivery of the child, even if from zina, fulfills the purpose of iddah. However, there is a nuance: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) hold that if the husband did not know of the pregnancy, the marriage is valid and iddah by delivery, but if he knew, the marriage is not valid. The eunuch (khasi) and the impotent man (majbub) are also discussed. A eunuch is like a normal man regarding iddah and lineage. An impotent man who can still ejaculate is like a normal man; if he cannot, he is like a minor regarding lineage but iddah still applies? The text says: 'The eunuch is like the sound man in lineage and iddah, likewise the man with severed genitals [majbub] if he ejaculates; if he does not ejaculate, the child does not belong to him, so he is like the minor regarding lineage and iddah.' Finally, the passage notes that a menstrual period in which divorce occurs is not counted in the iddah, because counting it would cause a reduction in the prescribed iddah (which is three full menstrual cycles). This is a ruling upon which there is consensus among the Hanafis, differing from Malik and Shafi'i who count that period if the divorce occurred during a state of purity.

Key Terms
الحامل من الزنا (al-hamil min al-zina): A woman who is pregnant as a result of adultery/fornication.
الخصي (al-khasi): A eunuch, a man whose testicles have been removed.
المجبوب (al-majbub): A man whose penis has been severed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the iddah of a woman who marries while pregnant from zina and is later divorced?
A) She must observe three menstrual cycles.
B) She must observe four months and ten days.
C) Her iddah ends with the delivery of the child.
D) She has no iddah because the pregnancy is from zina.

Key Takeaway
A woman pregnant from zina who marries another man ends her iddah by delivery; eunuchs and impotent men who can ejaculate are like normal men for iddah.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Iddah of the Wife of a Minor or Impotent Husband
Explains iddah and lineage rules for wife of minor/impotent, with disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a wife whose husband is a minor or impotent, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a woman pregnant from adultery who is divorced.

Core Idea
A wife of a minor or impotent man observes iddah by months, and lineage from such a husband is negated.

This lesson unpacks a dense passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that addresses the iddah of women married to men who cannot father children—minors, the impotent, and eunuchs. The central ruling is that such women observe iddah by three months, not by delivery or menstruation, and any child born is not attributed to the husband. The passage also delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) concerning a woman who was pregnant from adultery at the time of marriage and is later divorced. Understanding these nuances is vital for correctly applying iddah rules in cases where biological paternity is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Default Ruling
State that the act is the iddah of a wife whose husband is a minor (pre-pubescent) or impotent (majbub who does not emit). The ruling is that she observes iddah by three months (iddat al-shuhūr), and any child born to her during the marriage is not attributed to such a husband (lineage negated).
The basis is that a minor cannot produce semen, and an impotent man who does not emit is analogous.
2
Locate the Basis (ʿIllah) for the Ruling
The ruling is based on the fact that the husband cannot father a child. Therefore, the purpose of iddah—to ascertain paternity—is irrelevant. The iddah by months fulfills the requirement of a waiting period without the possibility of pregnancy.
This is a talīl (rationally deduced) argument; the ʿillah is the impossibility of impregnation.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute (Pregnant from Adultery)
Note the disagreement when a woman is pregnant from adultery at the time of marriage, then divorced or widowed. Abu Hanifa holds that her iddah is by months (since the pregnancy is not from the husband), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that she observes iddah by delivery (because the pregnancy becomes effective due to the marriage contract).
The text clarifies that this dispute applies only when the husband did not know of the premarital pregnancy. If he knew, the marriage is invalid according to Abu Yusuf.
4
Differentiate Related Cases (Eunuch, Impotent with Emission, Old Man)
A eunuch (khasi) and an impotent man who can emit semen are treated like a healthy man: lineage can be established and iddah is by delivery if a child is born. If the impotent man does not emit, he is like a minor. For an old man whose widow gives birth more than two years after his death, the Sahibayn consider her marriage valid if she remarries after four months and ten days, as she implicitly acknowledged the end of iddah.
These distinctions highlight the centrality of the possibility of impregnation.
5
Determine the Muftā Bihī (Operative Ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa in the case of a woman pregnant from adultery who marries and is divorced is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: she observes iddah by delivery. However, the main ruling (wife of minor/impotent observes by months) is settled within the school.
The text calls this the 'truth' (al-ḥaqq) and considers the contrary narration from Abu Hanifa as shādhdh (rare).

وإن كان من فجور والخصي كالصحيح في الولد والعدة وكذلك المجبوب إذا كان ينزل وإن لم ينزل لم يلزمه الولد فكان بمنزلة الصبي في الولد والعدة قوله : (ولم تعتد بحيض طلقت فيه للزوم النقص عن المقدر شرعاً لو اعتد بها وهذا بالإجماع بخلاف الطهر الذي وقع فيه الطلاق فإنه محسوب عند مالك والشافعي، وقد أورد عليهما لزوم النقصان عن
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Waiting Period (Iddah). Its placement and the pregnant woman [observe] by months, and lineage is negated in both, and she does not observe iddah by menstruation in which divorce occurred, and it becomes valid. He said: His ruling regarding iddah and child is the ruling of a healthy man. In al-Khaniyah, near the chapter of mahr: He married his slave-girl to a suckling infant, then she came with a child. The master claimed it, so the lineage was established because he acknowledged the lineage of one he owns and he has no known lineage. If the husband was impotent (majbub), lineage from the master is not established because it is established from the husband, and upon the husband is the entire mahr due to the consummation, as a legal ruling. End. The truth is that the statement of Abu Yusuf agrees with the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is only a rare narration from him agreeing with al-Shafi'i, and it is also a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) as verified in Fath al-Qadir. And in it: Based on this disagreement, if a man divorces his wife and she comes with a child not a miscarriage in less than six months from the time of the contract, such that he married her while she was pregnant from adultery, and the situation is not known, and she gave birth that way after divorce, she observes iddah by delivery according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to him (Abu Hanifa). We said 'and it is not known' to make it correct that this disagreement applies, because if he knew, then according to Abu Yusuf the contract would not be permissible because he prevents marriage to a woman pregnant from adultery, unlike when he does not know. For even though he does not validate it, he makes iddah obligatory due to the intercourse because it is a shubha (semblance of validity), so disagreement occurs whether it is by delivery or by months. End. In al-Bada'i': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said regarding the wife of an old man who gives birth after his death after more than two years, and she had married after the passing of four months and ten days: the marriage is valid because her entering into marriage is an acknowledgment from her of the expiration (of iddah) and nothing appears to invalidate that. He said: 'and lineage is negated in both,' i.e., in the one present at the time of death and the one occurring after, because the boy has no seminal fluid so impregnation cannot be imagined from him. The establishment of lineage of the child of the wife of the Eastern man from the Western woman is not refuted because we have established marriage in place of impregnation due to its actual possibility, which is not possible here in reality, so they differ. The apparent absolute application includes the adolescent, and it is appropriate to establish lineage as a precaution unless it is not possible, such as if she came with it in less than six months from the time of the contract as in Fath al-Qadir. For this issue, al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi includes cases such as when he was a suckling infant. Their speech regarding the wife of the minor indicates that the pregnant woman from adultery, if she marries and then her husband dies, her iddah is by delivery, as stated in al-Mi'raj citing Qadikhan. We have stated that a woman pregnant from adultery has no iddah according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and therefore they validate her marriage to someone other than the adulterer, though they prohibit intercourse. The discussion is regarding the case when she marries according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad while pregnant from adultery, then he divorces her or dies: then she observes iddah by delivery. In al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid, regarding the iddah of a minor's wife if she dies while pregnant: her iddah is by delivery. He said: because she died while pregnant, even if from fornication. The eunuch is like the healthy man regarding child and iddah, and similarly the impotent man if he emits; if he does not emit, the child is not attributed to him, so he is like the boy regarding child and iddah. He said: 'and she does not observe iddah by menstruation in which divorce occurred due to the necessary deficiency from what is legally prescribed if she were to observe it, and this is by consensus, unlike the pure period in which divorce occurred, for it is counted according to Malik and al-Shafi'i, and they have been objected to regarding the necessity of deficiency from
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings related to the iddah (waiting period) of a woman married to a minor or an impotent man, and the negation of lineage. The passage begins with a statement from the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) that the wife of a minor or an impotent man observes iddah by months (three months) rather than by delivery or menstruation, and that any child born is not attributed to such a husband because he cannot father a child. The author then expands on this, citing al-Khaniyah regarding a case where a master marries his slave-girl to a suckling infant (a minor): if she bears a child and both the master and the husband claim it, the child's lineage is established to the master if the husband has no known lineage, but if the husband is impotent (majbub), the lineage is not attributed to the master because it is already established to the husband. The passage then addresses a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a woman who is pregnant from adultery at the time of marriage: if the husband divorces her and she gives birth after less than six months from the contract, according to the two companions she observes iddah by delivery, while Abu Hanifa holds she observes by months. The author clarifies that the disagreement applies only when the husband did not know of the pregnancy; if he knew, the marriage is invalid according to Abu Yusuf. The passage also discusses the case of an old man whose wife gives birth after his death beyond two years, and then she remarries after four months and ten days: the two companions deem the second marriage valid because her action acknowledges the end of her iddah. The commentary further explains that lineage is negated in both the minor and the impotent because they cannot produce semen, and contrasts this with the case of a married couple from distant lands where lineage is established due to the possibility of consummation. Finally, the passage notes that a eunuch (khasi) and a masturbating impotent man (majbub who emits) are like a healthy man regarding child and iddah, but if the impotent man does not emit, he is like a minor. The discussion on the counting of iddah when divorce occurs in a menstrual period is also touched upon, with the author mentioning that according to the consensus, such a period is not counted, unlike the view of Malik and al-Shafi'i who count the pure period in which divorce occurred.

Key Terms
صبي (ṣabī): A minor male who has not reached puberty; in this context, unable to produce semen.
عدة الشهور (iddat al-shuhūr): The waiting period of three months prescribed for women who do not menstruate due to young age, old age, or absence of uterus.
مجبوب (majbūb): An impotent man whose penis is cut off or who cannot maintain an erection; if he also cannot emit, he is like a minor.
خصي (khaṣī): A eunuch; one whose testicles have been removed. He is treated like a healthy man regarding iddah and lineage because emission is still possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the default iddah for a wife whose husband is a minor (pre-pubescent) and no child is born?
A) Three months
B) Four months and ten days
C) Until she gives birth
D) Three menstrual cycles

Key Takeaway
A wife of a minor or impotent man who cannot emit observes iddah by three months, and any child born is not attributed to him.

Interlocking of Two Iddahs Due to Shubha Intercourse
Ruling and proof for interlocking of iddahs due to shubha intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on interlocking of two iddahs when a woman in iddah is penetrated under shubha and explain the rational proof (talil) based on the purpose of iddah.

Core Idea
Two compulsory iddahs interlock: the second is fulfilled within the first because the womb's emptiness is already established.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi family law: a woman currently observing iddah (waiting period) is sexually penetrated by another man under a semblance of legality (shubha). The Hanafi school rules that a second iddah becomes obligatory, but it 'interlocks' with the first, meaning no additional waiting time is needed. The reasoning is that the purpose of iddah is to ascertain the emptiness of the womb, which is already achieved by the first iddah. This principle distinguishes Hanafi law from other schools that may require separate sequential iddahs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a woman in iddah being penetrated under shubha. The ruling: a second iddah becomes wajib, but the two iddahs interlock.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas based on the purpose (talil): the purpose of iddah is to determine emptiness of womb. Once achieved by the first iddah, no further period is needed.
The text states the purpose explicitly: 'because the purpose is to determine the emptiness of the womb'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The analogy: just as a single iddah suffices to ascertain emptiness, a second iddah during the same period does not require extra time because the purpose is already fulfilled.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may require two separate consecutive iddahs. The Hanafi position relies on the primacy of purpose over form.
The text does not name the opposing school, but it is a known point of difference with the Shafi'i school.
5
Apply to scenarios
The text gives various examples: divorced woman remarries during iddah, then separation; pregnancy; different types of iddah (divorce vs death). In each, interlocking applies.
Study the examples to see how the principle is applied consistently.

He said: 'And another iddah becomes obligatory due to intercourse with a woman in iddah under shubha, and they interlock, and the visible of them, and the second completes if the first is completed.' Because the purpose is to determine the emptiness of the womb, and that has been obtained by one, so they interlock, and the aspect of worship in it is subsidiary. Do you not see that it ends without her knowledge and without her refraining from kuf (avoidance)? He made the intercourse under shubha absolute, so it includes the absolute divorcer and others. So if a divorced woman menstruated once, then married another and he had intercourse with her, then separation occurred between them, then she menstruated twice after separation: then the iddah of the first has expired, and it becomes permissible for the second to marry her, but it is not permissible for anyone else to marry her until she menstruates three times from the time of separation. If the first divorce was revocable, he may take her back before she menstruates twice due to the continuation of her iddah, but he may not have intercourse with her until the iddah of the second expires. If she menstruated three times from the time of separation, then both iddahs have expired. This is in al-Khaniyah. And intercourse under shubha occurs in scenarios: among them is a woman who is led to someone other than her husband; and among them is a woman who is penetrated by her husband after three divorces in a marriage before another husband; and during iddah if he says 'I thought she was permissible for me'; and among them is a woman who is irrevocably divorced by kinayah if he has intercourse with her during iddah; and among them is a woman in iddah if someone else has intercourse with her under shubha or within the marital bond, then the husband has intercourse with her under shubha then divorces her: in this case two iddahs become obligatory and they interlock. This is in Fath al-Qadir taken from al-Mi'raj of al-Yanabi', but he examines the issue of al-Mi'raj: the woman penetrated by her husband after three divorces if he claims a presumption of permissibility — it is of the type of shubha of act, and lineage is not established by that intercourse even if he says 'I thought she was permissible for me', and if lineage is not established then iddah is not obligatory. However, the latter case does not fall under the author's speech because his speech is about intercourse with a woman in iddah, and that is intercourse with a married woman, even though they share the obligation of two iddahs. His statement 'and the visible of them' is an explanation of the meaning of interlocking, but it is limited to one who menstruates, whereas his statement 'and they interlock' includes whether they are of the same kind (like intercourse with a woman in iddah from divorce) or of two kinds (like intercourse with a woman in iddah from death). As for one who does not menstruate, if two iddahs are obligatory, then the months for them are fulfilled in one period, whether in life or death. Similarly, a woman in iddah from death if she is penetrated under shubha observes iddah by months and counts what she sees of menstruation. So if she did not see any blood during it, she observes iddah after the months with three menstrual cycles, as in Fath al-Qadir. Two cases remain: if a non-pregnant woman in iddah of divorce or death is penetrated under shubha and becomes pregnant, then according to what is apparent in al-Mi'raj, interlocking occurs: so it expires by delivery because a pregnant woman does not menstruate according to us, so it is appropriate that delivery suffices. We have already mentioned this in the explanation of the iddah of the minor's wife citing al-Mujtaba, so refer to it. In Kafi al-Hakim: if a woman in iddah marries a man and he has intercourse with her and separation occurs between them, then if she was pregnant and delivered, both iddahs expire from both. Also in it: if she married during her iddah from a irrevocable divorce... al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.4, part 16.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling when a woman who is currently observing iddah (waiting period) is sexually penetrated by a man under shubha (a semblance of legal validity). The matn states that another iddah becomes obligatory, but the two iddahs interlock (tadakhul): meaning that the second iddah is fulfilled simultaneously with the first, so no extra time beyond the original iddah is needed. The reason given is that the purpose of iddah is to determine the emptiness of the womb, which is already achieved by the first iddah. The worship aspect of iddah is subsidiary; it ends even without the woman's knowledge or her refraining from avoidance. The author then expands by giving examples: if a divorced woman menstruates once, then marries another man and has intercourse, then separation occurs, she must complete the iddah of the first (if it was revocable) while also observing the iddah of the second. However, the second iddah is shorter because it starts after separation. The text details the scenarios of shubha intercourse: a woman mistakenly led to another man, a wife penetrated by her husband after three divorces before another marriage, a woman in iddah being penetrated by another man under false claim of permissibility, etc. A key point: if the husband penetrates his wife after three divorces claiming he thought she was permissible, this is shubha, but lineage is not established, and thus no iddah is required because the intercourse is not considered valid for establishing paternity. However, this case does not fall under the author's current discussion because the woman there is not in iddah; she is a married woman. The passage then discusses interlocking for women who do not menstruate: for them, if two iddahs are required (e.g., from divorce and death), they fulfill them by the same period of months. For a woman in iddah from death who is penetrated under shubha, she observes months and counts any menses; if no menses, she observes three months then three cycles. Finally, the case of a pregnant woman in iddah who is penetrated under shubha: she delivers and both iddahs expire with delivery, as pregnancy overrides menstruation. The commentary references various sources like al-Khaniyah, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Mi'raj.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadākhul): Interlocking or mutual inclusion: two iddahs are observed simultaneously, so the second is fulfilled within the period of the first.
وطء بشبهة (waṭʾ bi-shubha): Sexual intercourse that occurs under a semblance of legality, e.g., when one mistakenly believes the act is lawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The second iddah begins after the first ends
C) The second iddah is fulfilled within the period of the first, without adding extra time
D) The woman must choose which iddah to observe

Key Takeaway
When a woman in iddah is penetrated under shubha, the resulting second iddah interlocks with the first, requiring no extra time because the purpose of iddah—ascertaining womb emptiness—is already fulfilled.

Overlap of Waiting Periods for a Woman in Iddah Who Has Shubha Intercourse
States the ruling and gives a detailed example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on tadakhul (overlap) of iddahs and apply it to a scenario involving shubha intercourse during iddah.

Core Idea
When a woman in iddah has shubha intercourse, the second iddah overlaps with the first – one waiting period fulfills both.

When a woman is already observing iddah and then engages in sexual intercourse under a mistaken belief (shubha), Hanafi law imposes a second iddah. However, because the purpose of iddah is to know the emptiness of the womb, the two periods overlap – one menstruation-based waiting period counts for both. This lesson explains the ruling, its rationale, and its application through a detailed example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a woman already in iddah (e.g., after divorce) who has shubha intercourse. The ruling: a second iddah becomes wajib, but it overlaps with the first.
The ruling is stated in the matn: 'another waiting period is obligatory... and they overlap'.
2
Understand the rationale
The purpose of iddah is to ascertain emptiness of the womb (istibra' al-rahim). Since one waiting period achieves this, a second is not needed separately.
The commentator explains that the worship element (staying in the house) is subordinate to this purpose.
3
Apply the overlap to the example
Work through the example: a divorced woman menstruates once, remarries mistakenly, has intercourse, then separates. She menstruates twice after separation. The first iddah ends when she completes three menstruations from the time of the first divorce? Actually the example: she menstruated once before remarriage, then twice after separation. The first iddah is completed with the second menstruation? Need to track carefully. The example clarifies that the two menstruations after separation count for both iddahs.
The key: the blood seen (menstruation) is counted for both iddahs, and the second is completed if the first is completed.
4
Note the objection and response
An objection: if the first iddah is three menstruations and the second is also three, overlapping would reduce the number. The response: the deficiency is not considered because the purpose is achieved.
The matn's commentary addresses this: 'the deficient is not considered, neither at the beginning nor at the end'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدة أخرى بوطء المعتدة بشبهة وتداخلتا والمرئي منهما وتتم الثانية إن تمت الأولى ومبدأ الثلاثة فأورد علينا لزوم الزيادة عليها والخاص، كما لا يحتمل النقصان لا يحتمل الزيادة. وأجيب عنه بأنا لم نعتبر ذلك الزائد أصلاً فلا زيادة على الخاص والحاصل لا اعتبار بالناقص لا ابتداء ولا انتهاء . العدة قوله : (وتجب عدة أخرى بوطء المعتدة بشبهة وتداخلتا والمرئي منهما وتتم الثانية إن تمت الأولى) لأن المقصود التعرف عن فراغ الرحم وقد حصل بالواحدة فيتداخلان ومعنى العبادة فيها تابع ألا ترى أنها تنقضي بدون علمها ومن غير تركها الكف. أطلق الوطء بشبهة فشمل المطلق وغيره حتى لو حاضت المطلقة حيضة ثم تزوجت بآخر ووطئها، وفرق بينهما ثم حاضت حيضتين بعد التفريق فقد انقضت عدة الأول وحل للثاني أن يتزوجها وليس لغيره أن يتزوجها حتى تحيض ثلاثاً من وقت التفريق وإن كان طلاق الأول رجعياً كان له أن يراجعها قبل أن تحيض حيضتين لبقاء عدتها ولا يطؤها حتى تنقضي عدة الثاني، فإن حاضت ثلاثاً من وقت التفريق فقد انقضت العدتان؛ كذا في الخانية.
Another waiting period is obligatory by shubha intercourse with the woman in waiting period, and they overlap, and the blood seen is from both, and the second is completed if the first is completed, and the beginning of the three. So an objection was raised against us regarding the necessity of increase upon it and the specific, just as it does not admit deficiency, it does not admit increase. And it was answered that we did not consider that increase at all, so there is no increase on the specific, and the summary is that the deficient is not considered, neither at the beginning nor at the end. The waiting period. His statement: (And another waiting period is obligatory by shubha intercourse with the woman in waiting period, and they overlap, and the blood seen is from both, and the second is completed if the first is completed) because the purpose is to know the emptiness of the womb, and it has been achieved by one, so they overlap, and the meaning of worship in it is subordinate; do you not see that it expires without her knowledge and without her refraining from it. He made shubha intercourse general, so it includes the absolute divorce and other than it, even if the divorced woman menstruated once, then married another and he had intercourse with her, and they were separated, then she menstruated two times after the separation, then the waiting period of the first has expired, and it is permissible for the second to marry her, and no one else may marry her until she menstruates three times from the time of separation, and if the divorce of the first was revocable, he may take her back before she menstruates two times due to the continuance of her waiting period, and he may not have intercourse with her until the waiting period of the second expires. If she menstruates three times from the time of separation, then both waiting periods have expired; as in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, followed by its commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The ruling is that when a woman who is already in an iddah (waiting period) after divorce is subjected to sexual intercourse under a shubha (doubtful marriage, thinking it is permissible but it is not), a new iddah becomes obligatory for that intercourse. However, the two iddahs overlap (tadakhul), meaning she does not have to complete one after the other; instead, one period of waiting suffices for both. The blood she sees (menstruation) is counted for both iddahs, and the second iddah is completed only if the first is completed. The commentator explains the rationale: the purpose of iddah is to ascertain the emptiness of the womb after marital relations. When the womb is already known to be empty due to the first iddah, a second iddah for another intercourse is not needed separately—one period achieves the purpose. The worship aspect (of staying in the house) is subordinate to this purpose. He then gives an example: a divorced woman menstruates once, then marries another man and has intercourse. They are separated. She then menstruates twice after separation. The first iddah (of the first husband) expires, but she still has to complete three menstruations from the time of separation for the second husband. If the first husband had a revocable divorce, he can take her back before she completes two menstruations because her iddah with him is still ongoing, but he cannot have intercourse with her until the second iddah expires. If she menstruates three times from separation, both iddahs expire. This illustrates the overlap: the two menstruations after separation count for both the first and second iddahs, showing that they are completed together.

Key Terms
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarriage.
تداخل (tadākhul): Overlap of two waiting periods such that one period of waiting satisfies both.
وطء بشبهة (waṭʾ bi-shubhah): Sexual intercourse under a mistaken belief that the marriage is lawful, which actually is not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a divorced woman in her iddah has shubha intercourse with another man, how are the two iddahs fulfilled according to the Hanafi school?
A) She must complete the first iddah first, then start a new one.
B) They overlap, so one period of waiting suffices for both.
C) The second iddah cancels the first entirely.
D) She only observes one iddah from the second man.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a woman in iddah has shubha intercourse, the second iddah overlaps with the first – one waiting period suffices for both obligations.

Cases of Shubha Intercourse That Obligate Two Iddahs
Enumerates cases of shubha intercourse obligating two overlapping iddahs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific cases of shubha intercourse that obligate two overlapping iddahs according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Shubha intercourse during iddah can create two overlapping waiting periods.

This lesson enumerates the scenarios identified by Hanafi jurists where shubha (mistaken) intercourse gives rise to two iddahs that overlap. Understanding these cases is essential for correctly applying the rules of iddah in complex situations involving divorce, mistaken marriages, or conflicting claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common element
What unites all the cases? Each involves shubha intercourse that occurs while a woman is already in a state that would require an iddah (e.g., after divorce, during iddah, or mistaken marriage).
The shubha must be in the act itself (shubha fi'l-fi'l) or in the marriage (shubha fi'l-nikah).
2
List each case from the source
The passage enumerates four distinct cases. Write them down in order.
The second case (after three divorces) will be subject to scrutiny in the next lesson.
3
State the ruling
In each case, two iddahs become obligatory and they overlap (run concurrently).
Overlap means the woman completes both iddahs at the same time, not consecutively.
4
Note the exception
One case—the husband after three divorces claiming permissibility—is later questioned. Prepare to examine it in the next lesson.
The author of the source, Fath al-Qadir, takes the cases from al-Mi'raj, but then a scrutiny is mentioned.

والوطء بشبهة يتحقق في صور منها من زفت إلى غير زوجها، ومنها الموطوءة للزوج بعد الثلاث في بنكاح قبل زوج آخر، وفي العدة إذا قال ظننت أنها تحل لي ومنها المبانة في الكناية إذا وطئها في العدة، ومنها المعتدة إذا وطئها آخر في العدة بشبهة أو في عصمة فوطئها آخر بشبهة ثم طلقها الزوج ففي هذه تجب عدتان فيتداخلان؛ كذا في فتح القدير أخذاً من المعراج من الينابيع ولكنه نظر في مسألة المعراج وهي الموطوءة للزوج بعد الثلاث إذا ادعى ظن الحل بأنه من قبيل شبهة الفعل والنسب لا يثبت فيها بالوطء وإن قال ظننت أنها تحل لي، وإذا لم يثبت النسب لم تجب العدة لكن الأخيرة لم تدخل تحت كلام المصنف لأن كلامه في وطء المعتدة وتلك وطء المنكوحة وإن اشتركنا في وجوب عدتين قوله "والمرئي منهما" بيان لمعنى التداخل ولكنه قاصر على من تحيض بعد أن كان قوله "وتداخلتا" شاملاً لما إذا كانتا من جنس واحد كوطء المعتدة عن طلاق أو جنسين كوطء المعتدة عن وفاة وأما من لم تحض إذا وجبت عليها عدتان فالأشهر لهما يتأديان بمدة واحدة حياة ووفاة وكذا المعتدة عن وفاة إذا وطئت بشبهة تعتد بالشهور وتحتسب بما تراه من الحيض، فلو لم تر فيها دماً أن تعتد بعد الأشهر بثلاث حيض كما في فتح القدير. بقي صورتان لو كانت حائلاً في عدة الطلاق أو الموت فوطئت بشبهة فحبلت فظاهر ما في المعراج التداخل فتنقضي بوضع الحمل لأن الحامل لا تحيض عندنا فينبغي أن يكتفي بوضع الحمل وقد قدمنا في بيان عدة امرأة الصغير معزياً إلى المجتبى فارجع إليه.
And shubha intercourse is realized in images: among them is she who is taken to other than her husband, and among them is the woman who is subjected to intercourse by her husband after three divorces in a marriage before another husband, and in the waiting period if he said 'I thought she was permissible for me,' and among them is the woman who is separated by indirect expressions (kinaya) if he had intercourse with her in the waiting period, and among them is the woman in waiting period if another had intercourse with her in the waiting period under shubha, or in [his] guardianship, then another had intercourse with her under shubha, then the husband divorced her, so in these two waiting periods become obligatory and they overlap; as in Fath al-Qadir, taken from al-Mi'raj of al-Yanabi', but there is a scrutiny in the case of al-Mi'raj, which is the woman who is subjected to intercourse by her husband after three divorces if he claims presumption of permissibility, because it is of the category of shubha of act and lineage, it is not established by intercourse even if he said 'I thought she was permissible for me,' and if lineage is not established, the waiting period is not obligatory, but the latter [case] did not enter under the speech of the author because his speech is about shubha intercourse with the woman in waiting period, and that is intercourse with a married woman, even if we share in the obligation of two waiting periods. His statement 'and the blood seen is from both' is an explanation of the meaning of overlapping, but it is limited to those who menstruate, after his statement 'and they overlap' included when they are of the same type, like intercourse with a woman in iddah from divorce, or of two types, like intercourse with a woman in iddah from death. As for she who does not menstruate, if two waiting periods are obligatory for her, then the months for both are accomplished in one period, in life and death. Likewise, the woman in iddah from death if she is subjected to shubha intercourse, she observes iddah by months and counts what she sees of menstruation. So if she does not see blood in them, she must observe iddah after the months by three menstruations, as in Fath al-Qadir. Two cases remain: if she was not pregnant in the iddah of divorce or death, and was subjected to shubha intercourse and became pregnant, then the apparent from al-Mi'raj is overlapping, so it ends by delivery because the pregnant woman does not menstruate according to us, so it is appropriate that delivery suffices, and we have previously presented in the explanation of the iddah of the minor woman, attributed to al-Mujtaba, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the previous ruling, providing specific examples of shubha intercourse that trigger two iddahs. The commentator lists several cases: a woman brought to a man other than her husband (e.g., mistaken identity), a woman subjected to intercourse by her husband after three irrevocable divorces (if he mistakenly thinks it is lawful), a woman divorced by kinaya (indirect expressions) who is then subjected to intercourse during her iddah, and a woman in iddah who is subjected to shubha intercourse by one man and then another, and then her husband divorces her. In all these, two iddahs become obligatory and overlap. However, the commentator notes a scrutiny from al-Mi'raj: in the case where the husband has intercourse after three divorces claiming he thought it was permissible, this is considered shubha in act but not in lineage (nasab) because lineage is not established by such intercourse. Therefore, no iddah is obligatory because iddah is tied to the possibility of pregnancy (which affects lineage). This case, however, is not under the author's (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) statement because the author's statement is about a woman already in iddah, not about a married woman. The commentator then explains that the phrase 'the blood seen is from both' applies only to women who menstruate. The overlapping rule applies whether the two iddahs are of the same type (e.g., both from divorce) or different types (e.g., one from divorce and one from death). For a woman who does not menstruate (e.g., due to youth or old age), if two iddahs are obligatory, the months (for death iddah) and other periods are accomplished in one period. Similarly, a woman in iddah from death who has shubha intercourse observes iddah by months and counts any menstruation she sees; if she sees no blood, she must observe three menstruations after the months (because the months alone do not guarantee emptiness of womb). Finally, two cases remain: a non-pregnant woman in iddah (from divorce or death) who has shubha intercourse and becomes pregnant. The apparent ruling from al-Mi'raj is that the two iddahs overlap and end with delivery, since a pregnant woman does not menstruate (in the Hanafi view), so delivery suffices to know the womb is empty.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A mistaken belief that an act (here intercourse) is lawful when it is not, arising from ignorance or misinterpretation.
المبانة (al-mubānah): A woman divorced by irrevocable divorce (ba'in), especially via kinaya (indirect expressions).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a case where two overlapping iddahs are obligatory?
A) A woman mistakenly taken to another husband.
B) A woman divorced by kinaya and then subjected to shubha intercourse during iddah.
C) A husband who has intercourse with his wife after three divorces claiming permissibility.
D) A widow who remarries and then is divorced.

Key Takeaway
Shubha intercourse during iddah creates two overlapping waiting periods, except in the specific case of intercourse after three divorces where lineage is not established.

Scrutiny of Triple Divorce Intercourse and Overlap Rules for Non-Menstruating Women
Scrutinizes a specific case and explains overlap rules for non-menstruating and pregnant women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the scrutiny of the case of intercourse after three divorces and apply the rules for overlapping iddahs for non-menstruating and pregnant women.

Core Idea
Shubha in act alone does not establish lineage, thus no iddah is needed after triple divorce intercourse.

This lesson delves into the critical refinement of one of the previously listed cases: a husband who has intercourse with his wife after three irrevocable divorces, claiming he thought it was permissible. The commentator argues that this is shubha in act only, not sufficient to establish lineage, so no iddah is obligatory. The lesson also covers the application of overlapping rules for women who do not menstruate or who are pregnant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scrutiny
Why does the commentator question the earlier case? The case of intercourse after three divorces is considered shubha fi'l-fi'l (shubha in act) and does not establish lineage (nasab).
Iddah is tied to the possibility of pregnancy and lineage. Without lineage, no iddah.
2
Analyze the distinction
Distinguish between shubha fi'l-fi'l (act) and shubha fi'l-nikah (marriage).
Here, the shubha is in the act, not in the marriage. The husband knows the marriage ended.
3
Apply overlapping rule to non-menstruating women
If a woman does not menstruate (e.g., young, old), the months for both iddahs run concurrently.
For example, a 70-year-old woman in iddah from divorce and subject to shubha intercourse would observe 3 months for both iddahs simultaneously.
4
Handle women in iddah from death
A woman in iddah from death who has shubha intercourse counts months and any menstruation. If no blood, she observes 3 menstrual periods after the months.
This ensures the womb is empty.
5
Address pregnancy
If a non-pregnant woman in iddah becomes pregnant due to shubha intercourse, her iddah ends with delivery.
Pregnant women do not menstruate (Hanafi view), so delivery is sufficient to prove emptiness.

Child Attribution in Overlapping Iddahs: The Hanafi Position
Rulings on child lineage from Kafi al-Hakim, with author's additions and dispute with al-Bada'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for attributing a child when a woman marries another during her iddah and gives birth, applying the two-year and six-month thresholds.

Core Idea
A child born during overlapping iddahs is attributed based on maximum pregnancy (2 years) and minimum (6 months).

When a woman in iddah marries another man (an invalid marriage) and becomes pregnant, determining the child's lineage becomes complex. Hanafi fiqh uses two key time limits: the maximum pregnancy duration (two years) and the minimum (six months). This lesson unpacks the four possible scenarios from Kafi al-Hakim and the preferred view in the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
The woman is in iddah from a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, marries another, has intercourse, and is later separated. She gives birth during overlapping iddahs.
The first divorce is ba'in, so the iddah is three menstrual cycles or delivery.
2
Apply the two-year rule from first divorce
If the child is born less than two years from the date of the first divorce, it could be from the first husband (since Hanafi maximum pregnancy is two years). If more than two years, it is impossible to be from the first.
Maximum pregnancy in Hanafi: 2 years, based on hadith and practice.
3
Apply the six-month rule from second marriage
If the child is born less than six months from the second husband's intercourse, it cannot be from him (minimum pregnancy is six months). If six months or more, it could be from the second.
Minimum pregnancy: 6 months, based on Quran 46:15 and 31:14.
4
Determine attribution based on both thresholds
Combine the two rules: (a) less than 2 yrs from first & less than 6 months from second: child to first. (b) more than 2 yrs from first & less than 6 months from second: to neither. (c) less than 2 yrs & more than 6 months: to first. (d) more than 2 yrs & more than 6 months: to second.
The author adds cases (c) and (d) to the Kafi al-Hakim's original two.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Bada'i held that in case (c) the child is to the second, arguing that her marriage indicates iddah expiry. The author prefers Kafi al-Hakim (child to first) because the marriage is fasid and the iddah may not have ended.
The mufta bihi is the later view (Kafi al-Hakim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كافي الحاكم: لو تزوجت المعتدة برجل ودخل بها وفرق بينهما، فإن كانت حاملاً فوضعت انتقضت العدتان منهما جميعاً. وفيه أيضاً لو تزوجت في عدتها من طلاق بائن ودخل بها فولدت لأقل من سنتين منذ طلق الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر منذ دخل الثاني لزم الأول، وإن كان لأكثر من سنتين منذ طلقها الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر منذ دخل الثاني لم يلزم الأول ولا الثاني. اهـ. بقي ما لو جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من دخول الثاني وينبغي إلحاقه بالأول، وبقي ما لو جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين من طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من دخول الثاني، ولا شك بإلحاقه بالثاني فهي رباعية. وفي نسختي الكافي للحاكم الشهيد سقط وتغيير في هذا المحل وفي الجوهرة: ثم إذا تداخلتا عندنا وكانت العدة من طلاق رجعي فلا نفقة على واحد منهما لها وإن كانت من بائن فنفقتها على الأول والزوجة إذا تزوجت بآخر وفرق بينهما بعد الدخول ووجبت عليها العدة فلا نفقة لها هذه العدة على زوجها لأنها منعت نفسها في العدة. اهـ. فعلى هذا فالمنع الشرعي أقوى من المنع الحسي لأنها لو منعته عن جماعها لها النفقة. وفي المجتبى: كل نكاح اختلف العلماء في جوازه كالنكاح بلا شهود فالدخول فيه يوجب العدة، أما نكاح منكوحة الغير ومعتدته فالدخول فيه لا يوجب العدة إن علم أنها للغير لأنه لم يقل أحد بجوازه فلم ينعقد أصلاً فعلى هذا يفرق بين فاسده وباطله في الحد العدة ولهذا يجب مع العلم بالحرمة لكونه زنا كما في القنية وغيرها، ولو كان الواطىء في العدة والمطلق هو فلا نفقة لها بعد عدة الطلاق؛ كذا في المجتبى. ثم اعلم أن المرئي إنما يكون منهما إذا كان بعد التفريق بينها وبين الواطىء الثاني، أما إذا حاضت حيضة بعد وطء الثاني قبل التفريق فإنها من عدة الأول خاصة وبقي عليها من تمام عدة الأول حيضتان وللثاني ثلاث حيض، فإذا حاضت حيضتين كانت منهما جميعاً وبقيت من عدة الثاني حيضة؛ كذا في الجوهرة. فإن قيل: إذا كان الواطىء المطلق فهل يشترط أن يكون بعد التفريق أيضاً قلت لم أره صريحاً وفي الولوالجية: رجل طلق امرأته ثلاثاً فلما اعتدت بحيضتين أكرهها على الجماع فإن جامعها منكراً طلاقها تستقبل العدة، وإن كان مقراً بطلاقها لكن جامعها على وجه الزنا لا تستقبل، وكذلك من طلق امرأته ثم أقام معها زماناً فعلى التفصيل. اهـ. وشمل قوله المعتدة عن وطء بشبهة لو وطئت. قوله: (وينبغي الحاقه بالأول سيأتي في أوائل ثبوت النسب عن البدائع أنه للثاني في هذه الصورة وأن نكاح الثاني جائز لأن إقدامها على التزوج دليل انقضاء عدتها من الأول اهـ. لكن راجعت كافي الحاكم فرأيته ذكر ما يوافق بحث المؤلف وعبارته هكذا وإن تزوجت المرأة في عدتها من طلاق بائن ودخل بها زوجها فجاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين من يوم طلقها الأول ولستة أشهر أو أكثر منذ تزوجها الآخر فالولد للأول لأن نكاح الآخر كان فاسداً وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين منذ طلق الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر منذ تزوجها الآخر لم يلزم الأول ولا الآخر لأن النساء لا يلدن لأكثر من سنتين ولا يلدن لأقل من ستة أشهر، وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين منذ طلقها الأول ولستة أشهر منذ تزوجها الآخر ودخل بها فهو للآخر.
And in Kafi al-Hakim: If a woman in iddah married a man and he had intercourse with her and they were separated, and if she was pregnant and gave birth, both iddahs from both of them expire. And also in it: If she married during her iddah from a ba'in divorce and he had intercourse with her, and she gave birth to a child less than two years from the day the first husband divorced her and less than six months from the day the second husband had intercourse with her, [the child] is attributed to the first. If it was more than two years from the time the first husband divorced her and less than six months from the time the second had intercourse, it is not attributed to either the first or the second. End. There remains the case if she produced it less than two years from the divorce of the first and six months from the intercourse of the second, and it is fitting to attribute it to the first; and there remains the case if she produced it more than two years from the divorce of the first and six months from the intercourse of the second, and there is no doubt in attributing it to the second, so it is fourfold. And in the two copies of Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid, there is omission and change in this place. And in al-Jawhara: Then if they overlap according to us, and the iddah was from a revocable divorce, then there is no nafaqah for her from either one of them; if it was from a ba'in divorce, then her nafaqah is upon the first. And the wife, if she married another and was separated after intercourse and a waiting period became obligatory for her, there is no nafaqah for her in this waiting period upon her husband because she prevented herself during the iddah. End. According to this, the legal prevention is stronger than the physical prevention, because if she prevents him from intercourse, she has nafaqah. And in al-Mujtaba: Every marriage whose permissibility is disputed by scholars, like marriage without witnesses, then intercourse in it obligates a waiting period. As for the marriage of another's wife or a woman in iddah, then intercourse in it does not obligate a waiting period if he knows she belongs to another, because no one said it is permissible, so it did not contract at all. According to this, a distinction is made between its fasid (corrupt) and batil (void) in the limit of the waiting period, and for this it is obligatory despite knowledge of prohibition, because it is zina, as in al-Qunyah and others. And if the one having intercourse in the iddah and the one divorcing are the same, then there is no nafaqah for her after the iddah of divorce; as in al-Mujtaba. Then know that the blood seen is from both only if it is after separation between her and the second occupant. As for if she menstruated once after the intercourse of the second before separation, then it is from the iddah of the first specifically, and there remains on her from the completion of the iddah of the first two menstruations, and for the second three menstruations. If she menstruated two times, they are from both, and there remains from the iddah of the second one menstruation; as in al-Jawhara. If it is said: If the occupant is the divorcing husband, is it a condition that it be after separation as well? I said: I did not find it explicitly. And in al-Walwaljiyya: A man divorced his wife three times, and when she had observed two menstruations, he forced her to have intercourse; if he had intercourse denying her divorce, she restarts the waiting period; if he acknowledged the divorce but had intercourse with her as zina, she does not restart. Likewise, whoever divorces his wife and then lives with her for a time, it is with detail. End. And his statement includes the woman in iddah from shubha intercourse if she was subjected to intercourse. His statement: 'And it is fitting to attribute it to the first' will come in the beginning of the establishment of lineage from al-Bada'i that it is for the second in this situation, and that the marriage of the second is permissible because her initiating marriage is evidence of the expiration of her iddah from the first, end. But I consulted Kafi al-Hakim and saw that he mentioned what agrees with the author's research, and his phrase is as follows: If a woman married during her iddah from a ba'in divorce and her husband had intercourse with her, and she gave birth to a child less than two years from the day her first husband divorced her and six months or more from the day she married the other, then the child is for the first because the marriage of the other was fasid. If she gave birth to it more than two years from the day the first divorced and less than six months from the day she married the other, then it is not attributed to the first nor to the other because women do not give birth after more than two years and do not give birth before six months. If she gave birth to it more than two years from the day her first divorced her and six months from the day she married the other and he had intercourse, then it is for the other.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage covers multiple interrelated rulings when a woman marries another man during her iddah (which is invalid) and has intercourse, leading to overlapping iddahs. The Kafi al-Hakim states that if she was pregnant and gave birth, both iddahs end with delivery. For child attribution, if the child is born within two years of the first divorce (the maximum duration of pregnancy according to Hanafi view) and within six months of the second marriage, the child is attributed to the first husband. If more than two years from the first divorce but less than six months from the second, the child is not attributed to either, as it falls outside the possible pregnancy periods. The commentator adds two missing cases: if between the two year and six month mark, it should be to the first; if beyond both, to the second. Regarding nafaqah (maintenance), if the two iddahs overlap and the original divorce was revocable (raj'i), then neither husband owes maintenance during the overlapping period because she is still considered married to the first and the second marriage is invalid. If the original divorce was ba'in (irrevocable), the first husband owes maintenance. However, if a woman marries another and is separated, no maintenance is owed during this iddah because she prevented herself from the real husband. Legal prevention (due to iddah prohibition) is stronger than physical prevention (e.g., locking oneself). The passage then distinguishes between fasid (corrupt) and batil (void) marriages regarding iddah: if a marriage is disputed but considered possibly valid (like without witnesses), intercourse obligates iddah; if it is certainly void (like marriage to another's wife), intercourse does not obligate iddah because it is zina. Finally, it discusses the timing of blood seen: if she menstruates after separation from the second, that blood counts for both iddahs; if before separation, it counts only for the first. If the same man (the original husband) is the one having intercourse during her iddah, the rulings differ based on intent. A gloss from al-Bada'i contrasts with Kafi al-Hakim on child attribution, but the author sides with Kafi al-Hakim's view.

Key Terms
النسب (al-nasab): The legal lineage or paternity of a child, determining which man is the father.
فاسد (fāsid): A contract that is defective but may produce some legal effects, such as the need for iddah after intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in iddah from a ba'in divorce marries another and gives birth 2 years and 3 months after the first divorce and 7 months after the second marriage. Under Hanafi law, to whom is the child attributed?
A) The first husband
B) The second husband
C) Neither husband
D) It depends on a court ruling

Key Takeaway
Child attribution in overlapping iddahs is determined by two fixed time thresholds: the two-year maximum and the six-month minimum, yielding four possible outcomes.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Nafaqah and Marriage Validity in Overlapping Iddahs
Rulings on nafaqah, the fasid/batil distinction, and blood timing from al-Jawhara and al-Mujtaba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the nafaqah obligations when iddahs overlap and distinguish between fasid and batil marriages regarding the obligation of iddah.

Core Idea
In overlapping iddahs, nafaqah depends on divorce type; fasid marriage obligates iddah, but batil does not.

When a woman is in iddah from one marriage and enters another invalid marriage, questions arise about maintenance and the legal status of the second union. This lesson explores the rules for nafaqah in overlapping iddahs and the critical distinction between fasid (corrupt) and batil (void) marriages in whether intercourse triggers a waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of first divorce
Determine whether the original divorce was revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). This affects who owes nafaqah if iddahs overlap.
Revocable: husband can take wife back; ba'in: marriage is permanently dissolved.
2
Apply nafaqah rule for overlapping iddahs
From al-Jawhara: If the original divorce is revocable, then in overlapping iddahs, neither husband owes nafaqah. If ba'in, the first husband owes nafaqah.
Reason for revocable: she is still considered the first wife during iddah, but since she prevented herself (by remarrying), the first husband is not liable.
3
Understand legal prevention vs physical prevention
Legal prevention (shar'i) from intercourse (due to iddah) is stronger than physical prevention (like locking oneself). If she prevents physically, she loses nafaqah; if legally, she still gets it? Actually, the passage says legal prevention (being in iddah) does not prevent nafaqah from the first if he is the one who put her in iddah.
The principle: 'al-man' al-shar'i aqwa min al-man' al-hissi' (legal prevention is stronger than physical prevention).
4
Distinguish fasid and batil marriages
If the second marriage is disputed (like without witnesses), it is fasid and intercourse obligates a new iddah. If it is completely void (like marrying a married woman), it is batil and intercourse does not obligate iddah—it is zina.
Fasid contracts can produce some legal effects; batil contracts are null ab initio.
5
Apply the blood timing rule
If she menstruates after separation from the second, that menstruation counts for both iddahs. If before separation, it counts only for the first.
This affects how many days remain in each iddah.

Commentary
This passage covers multiple interrelated rulings when a woman marries another man during her iddah (which is invalid) and has intercourse, leading to overlapping iddahs. The Kafi al-Hakim states that if she was pregnant and gave birth, both iddahs end with delivery. For child attribution, if the child is born within two years of the first divorce (the maximum duration of pregnancy according to Hanafi view) and within six months of the second marriage, the child is attributed to the first husband. If more than two years from the first divorce but less than six months from the second, the child is not attributed to either, as it falls outside the possible pregnancy periods. The commentator adds two missing cases: if between the two year and six month mark, it should be to the first; if beyond both, to the second. Regarding nafaqah (maintenance), if the two iddahs overlap and the original divorce was revocable (raj'i), then neither husband owes maintenance during the overlapping period because she is still considered married to the first and the second marriage is invalid. If the original divorce was ba'in (irrevocable), the first husband owes maintenance. However, if a woman marries another and is separated, no maintenance is owed during this iddah because she prevented herself from the real husband. Legal prevention (due to iddah prohibition) is stronger than physical prevention (e.g., locking oneself). The passage then distinguishes between fasid (corrupt) and batil (void) marriages regarding iddah: if a marriage is disputed but considered possibly valid (like without witnesses), intercourse obligates iddah; if it is certainly void (like marriage to another's wife), intercourse does not obligate iddah because it is zina. Finally, it discusses the timing of blood seen: if she menstruates after separation from the second, that blood counts for both iddahs; if before separation, it counts only for the first. If the same man (the original husband) is the one having intercourse during her iddah, the rulings differ based on intent. A gloss from al-Bada'i contrasts with Kafi al-Hakim on child attribution, but the author sides with Kafi al-Hakim's view.

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance owed by the husband to the wife during the waiting period, including food, clothing, and housing.
المنع الشرعي (al-man' al-shar'i): Legal prevention: a woman's inability to have intercourse due to a legal impediment (like iddah).
فاسد / باطل (fāsid / bāṭil): Fasid: a contract that is defective but can be corrected (e.g., marriage without witnesses). Baṭil: void, no legal effect (e.g., marriage to a mahram).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The second husband alone must provide nafaqah because the marriage is invalid.
C) Neither husband provides nafaqah.
D) Both husbands share the nafaqah equally.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah rules in overlapping iddahs hinge on the nature of the first divorce, and the fasid/batil distinction determines whether a waiting period from the second marriage is required.

Paternity in Remarriage During 'Iddah: Four Scenarios
Expansion of paternity scenarios for remarriage during 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi paternity rules to any combination of divorce and remarriage timings and determine which husband is the father.

Core Idea
Paternity is determined by two fixed durations: 6 months minimum and 2 years maximum pregnancy.

When a divorced woman remarries during her 'iddah and gives birth, determining the father requires precise calculation. The Hanafi school uses the well-established rules of minimum pregnancy (6 months) and maximum pregnancy (2 years) to assign paternity. This lesson walks through all four logical scenarios, including two that the original matn left implicit but are necessary for complete coverage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two timelines
We have two starting points: the date of divorce from the first husband and the date of the second husband's entry (consummation). Both are measured against the birth date.
These are the only relevant events for paternity assignment.
2
Apply the minimum pregnancy rule
A viable child must have been conceived at least 6 months before birth. If born less than 6 months after second husband's entry, the child cannot be his.
Six months is derived from Qur'an 46:15 and Hanafi principles.
3
Apply the maximum pregnancy rule
Pregnancy cannot exceed 2 years in the Hanafi school. If born more than 2 years after divorce, the child cannot be the first husband's.
Two years is based on the practice of the Companions and istihsan.
4
Combine the rules to assign paternity
Only one husband can be the father based on the overlapping windows. If both windows close, paternity is suspended.
The added scenarios (3 and 4) cover the edge cases at exactly 6 months from the second husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل بها فولدت لأقل من سنتين منذ طلق الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر منذ دخل الثاني لزم الأول، وإن كان لأكثر من سنتين منذ طلقها الأول ولأقل من ستة أشهر منذ دخل الثاني لم يلزم الأول ولا الثاني .اهـ. بقي ما لو جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من دخول الثاني وينبغي إلحاقه بالأول، وبقي ما لو جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين من طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من دخول الثاني، ولا شك بإلحاقه بالثاني فهي رباعية.
And if he entered her and she gave birth to a child at less than two years from the first husband's divorce and at less than six months from the second husband's entry, the paternity is assigned to the first. And if it was more than two years from the first husband's divorce and less than six months from the second husband's entry, paternity is not assigned to either. (End of quote) There remains the case if she brought it at less than two years from the first husband's divorce and six months from the second's entry; it should be attached to the first. And there remains the case if she brought it at more than two years from the first's divorce and six months from the second's entry; there is no doubt in attaching it to the second, so it is fourfold.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on paternity (nasab) when a woman is divorced by the first husband, then marries another during her 'iddah, and gives birth. The ruling is based on the minimum pregnancy duration (six months) and the maximum (two years) according to the Hanafi school. Four scenarios are enumerated: (1) birth 2 years from first divorce and 2 years from first divorce and exactly 6 months from second marriage → paternity to second husband. The passage is from the sharh (expansion probe) explaining the matn's ruling and adding two cases that were not explicitly stated.

Key Terms
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarriage.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity; the legal attribution of a child to its father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the four scenarios for paternity, if a woman gives birth at exactly 6 months after the second husband's entry and less than 2 years after the first divorce, to whom is the child attributed?
A) The first husband
B) The second husband
C) Neither husband
D) It is disputed

Key Takeaway
Paternity in remarriage during 'iddah is determined by the intersection of two legal presumptions: the minimum pregnancy of 6 months and the maximum pregnancy of 2 years.

Nafaqah When Two ‘Iddahs Overlap
Expands the ruling on nafaqah from al-Jawharah and al-Mujtaba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a woman in overlapping ‘iddahs and distinguish between revocable and irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
In overlapping ‘iddahs, nafaqah depends on the type of divorce: revocable → none; irrevocable → first husband pays.

Sometimes a divorced woman remarries during her ‘iddah, creating two overlapping waiting periods. Who must support her? This lesson extracts the Hanafi rule from the commentaries of Ibn Nujaym and others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overlapping ‘iddah scenario
Recognize that two ‘iddahs occur when a woman in ‘iddah from one divorce contracts a second marriage (fasid) and consummates it.
2
Classify the divorce type
Determine if the original divorce is revocable (raj‘i) or irrevocable (ba’in). The ruling changes accordingly.
Revocable: husband can take her back without remarriage.
3
Apply the nafaqah rule
If revocable: no nafaqah from either husband. If irrevocable: nafaqah is on the first husband only.
The text states: 'If the ‘iddah is from a revocable divorce, then there is no nafaqah on either of them for her. But if it is from an irrevocable divorce, then her nafaqah is on the first husband.'
4
Understand the reasoning
The legal prevention (being in ‘iddah) is stronger than physical prevention (denying intercourse). In revocable divorce, she is still under the first husband's authority but the second marriage is invalid, so neither owes nafaqah.
The passage notes: 'legal prevention is stronger than physical prevention.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نسختي الكافي للحاكم الشهيد سقط وتغيير في هذا المحل وفي الجوهرة: ثم إذا تداخلتا عندنا وكانت العدة من طلاق رجعي فلا نفقة على واحد منهما لها وإن كانت من بائن فنفقتها على الأول والزوجة إذا تزوجت بآخر وفرق بينهما بعد الدخول ووجبت عليها العدة فلا نفقة لها هذه العدة على زوجها لأنها منعت نفسها في العدة .اهـ. فعلى هذا فالمنع الشرعي أقوى من المنع الحسي لأنها لو منعته عن جماعها لها النفقة. وفي المجتبى : كل نكاح اختلف العلماء في جوازه کالنكاح بلا شهود فالدخول فيه يوجب العدة، أما نكاح منكوحة الغير ومعتدته فالدخول فيه لا يوجب العدة إن علم أنها للغير لأنه لم يقل أحد بجوازه فلم ينعقد أصلاً فعلى هذا يفرق بين فاسده وباطله في الحد العدة ولهذا يجب مع العلم بالحرمة لكونه زنا كما في القنية وغيرها، ولو كان الواطىء في العدة والمطلق هو فلا نفقة لها بعد عدة الطلاق؛ كذا في المجتبى . ثم اعلم أن المرئي إنما يكون منهما إذا كان بعد التفريق بينها وبين الواطىء الثاني، أما إذا حاضت حيضة بعد وطء الثاني قبل التفريق فإنها من عدة الأول خاصة وبقي عليها من تمام عدة الأول حيضتان وللثاني ثلاث حيض، فإذا حاضت حيضتين كانت منهما جميعاً وبقيت من عدة الثاني حيضة؛ كذا في الجوهرة. فإن قيل : إذا كان الواطىء المطلق فهل يشترط أن يكون بعد التفريق أيضاً قلت لم أره صريحاً وفي الولوالجية: رجل طلق امرأته ثلاثاً فلما اعتدت بحيضتين أكرهها على الجماع فإن جامعها منكراً طلاقها تستقبل العدة، وإن كان مقراً بطلاقها لكن جامعها على وجه الزنا لا تستقبل، وكذلك من طلق امرأته ثم أقام معها زماناً فعلى التفصيل .اهـ. وشمل قوله المعتدة عن وطء بشبهة لو وطئت
And in the two copies of al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid, there is a deletion and change in this place, and in al-Jawharah: Then if the two 'iddahs overlap according to us, and the 'iddah is from a revocable divorce, then there is no nafaqah on either of them for her. But if it is from an irrevocable divorce, then her nafaqah is on the first husband. And if a wife marries another and they are separated after consummation and 'iddah becomes obligatory for her, then she has no nafaqah during this 'iddah from her husband because she prevented herself in the 'iddah. (End quote) Accordingly, the legal prevention is stronger than the physical prevention, because if she prevented him from intercourse, she would have nafaqah. And in al-Mujtaba: Every marriage about which scholars differed regarding its permissibility—like marriage without witnesses—then consummation therein obligates 'iddah. As for the marriage of another's wife or one in 'iddah, consummation therein does not obligate 'iddah if he knew she was for another, because no one said it is permissible, so it was not valid at all. Accordingly, a distinction is made between its fasid and batil regarding the limit of 'iddah. And for this reason, it is obligatory with knowledge of prohibition because it is zina, as in al-Qunyah and others. And if the one who has intercourse during her 'iddah is the husband who divorced, then she has no nafaqah after the 'iddah of divorce; thus in al-Mujtaba. Then know that the menstrual blood counted is from both only if it is after the separation between her and the second one who had intercourse. But if she menstruated once after the second's intercourse before separation, then it is from the first's 'iddah only, and there remains for her from the completion of the first's 'iddah two menstruations, and for the second three menstruations. So if she menstruated twice, they are from both, and there remains from the second's 'iddah one menstruation; thus in al-Jawharah. If it is said: If the one who had intercourse is the husband who divorced, is it also required that it be after separation? I said: I have not seen it explicitly. And in al-Walwaljiyah: A man divorced his wife three times, and when she had completed two menstruations of her 'iddah, he forced her into intercourse. If he had intercourse denying his divorce, she must start the 'iddah anew. But if he acknowledged his divorce but had intercourse as adultery, she does not start anew. Similarly, if a man divorced his wife and then stayed with her for a time, the details are as mentioned. (End quote) And his statement includes: The one in 'iddah from intercourse under shubha if she is subject to intercourse...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses several issues related to overlapping 'iddahs. First, it discusses the case when a woman is divorced and then remarries during her 'iddah, resulting in two 'iddahs. If the divorce was revocable (raj‘i), neither husband owes her nafaqah because she is still under the first husband's authority but the second marriage is invalid. If the divorce was irrevocable (ba’in), the first husband must pay nafaqah. The author then explains that preventing oneself legally (by being in 'iddah) is different from physical prevention: if she physically prevented him from intercourse, she would still get nafaqah, but here she legally prevented herself. The passage then discusses which marriages obligate 'iddah upon consummation: marriages that are disputed (e.g., without witnesses) do, but marriages that are universally invalid (e.g., marrying another man's wife knowingly) do not, as they are considered pure zina. The distinction between fasid (defective) and batil (void) marriage is clarified regarding 'iddah. Finally, it details how to count menstruations when a woman has intercourse with the second husband before the separation: only one 'iddah counts until separation, after which both 'iddahs can run concurrently. The last part deals with a husband who forces intercourse during her 'iddah: if he denies the divorce, the 'iddah starts again; if he acknowledges divorce but commits zina, it does not restart.

Key Terms
تداخل العدة (tadakhul al-‘iddah): The overlapping of two 'iddah periods when a woman remarries during her 'iddah.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance or financial support provided to a woman during her 'iddah.
منع شرعي (man‘ shar‘i): Legal prevention, e.g., being in 'iddah which prohibits marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of divorce results in nafaqah being due from the first husband during overlapping 'iddahs?
A) Revocable divorce
B) Irrevocable divorce
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah in overlapping 'iddahs hinges on whether the divorce is revocable (no nafaqah) or irrevocable (first husband pays).

Which Marriages Obligate ‘Iddah: Fasid vs Batil
Expands the distinction from al-Mujtaba and al-Jawharah on fasid vs batil marriages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid and batil marriages regarding ‘iddah obligation and apply the Hanafi rule on counting menstruations in overlapping ‘iddahs.

Core Idea
A consummated marriage that scholars differ on obligates ‘iddah; one universally invalid does not — it is pure zina.

When a second marriage during ‘iddah is invalid, we must determine if it obligates a separate ‘iddah. The Hanafi school distinguishes between marriages that are merely defective (fasid) and those that are void (batil). This lesson explores that distinction and its practical application in counting menstruations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second marriage type
Determine if the marriage is fasid (disputed permissibility) or batil (universally prohibited, e.g., marrying a woman known to be another's wife).
2
Apply the ‘iddah rule
If fasid and consummated: ‘iddah is obligatory. If batil and consummated: no ‘iddah; it is zina.
Passage: 'Every marriage about which scholars differed... consummation obligates ‘iddah. As for marriage of another's wife... consummation does not obligate ‘iddah.'
3
Distinguish fasid from batil
In Hanafi fiqh, fasid means the contract is flawed but not entirely void; batil means it is void ab initio. Only fasid requires ‘iddah.
4
Understand counting of menstruations
If a woman has intercourse with the second husband before separation, the first menstruation counts only for the first ‘iddah. After separation, subsequent menstruations count for both.
Passage details: if she menstruates once before separation, it's for first; if twice, it's for both.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage contract that is defective due to missing a condition (like witnesses) but not a pillar; its validity is disputed.
نكاح باطل (nikah batil): A marriage contract that is void ab initio, e.g., marrying a woman known to be another's wife.
شبهة (shubha): Resemblance or doubt about validity; here, the semblance of a valid marriage due to scholarly disagreement.
